Dialectic -- Jadal

Classifying Mu'arada: Target and Method in Dialectical Opposition
Author classifies mu'arada into two types by target and three types by method, yielding six total combinations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any instance of mu'arada into one of the six types by identifying its target (proof or cause) and method (reversal, like, or other).

Core Idea
Every mu'arada has a target and a method; multiplying them yields six precise types.

In dialectical debate (munazara), knowing how to oppose an argument systematically is essential. This lesson introduces the classification of mu'arada (opposition) according to two dimensions: what it targets (the proof itself or the reasoning behind it) and how it is executed (by reversing the argument, bringing similar evidence, or bringing different evidence). Understanding this grid prepares you to both mount and defend against structured objections.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two target types
Mu'arada can target either the dalil (evidence) or the 'illa (the cause/reason). Attacking the proof means challenging the premises, while attacking the cause means challenging the rationale.
2
Understand the three methods
Qalb (reversal): using the opponent's own evidence against him. Mithl (like): providing analogous evidence. Ghayr (other): providing evidence of a different kind.
3
Combine target and method
Each method can be applied to each target, so the total types are 2 × 3 = 6. Practice thinking: can you have a reversal of the cause? Yes.
4
Identify examples
The author promises examples later, but we can construct a simple one: If someone argues that wine is forbidden because it intoxicates, mu'arada fi al-'illa bi'l-mithl would be: 'But khamr is forbidden because of intoxication; nabidh also intoxicates, so it is also forbidden.' That is a mu'arada in the cause by like.
5
Apply the classification
Given an argument, determine the target and method of a mu'arada. For instance, if an opponent says: 'Your proof that the world is temporal is weak because it relies on the assumption of change,' this is mu'arada fi al-dalil bi'l-ghayr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن المعارضة عند أهل هذا الفن تختلف تقسيماتها باختلاف الاعتبارات، فتنقسم باعتبار ما توجه إليه إلى قسمين: الأول يسمى المعارضة في الدليل والثاني يسمى المعارضة في العلة . وتنقسم باعتبار مقارنة دليل المعارض بدليل المعلل إلى ثلاثة أقسام الأول المعارضة على سبيل القلب، والثاني المعارضة بالمثل، والثالث المعارضة بالغير، وكل واحد من هذه الأنواع الثلاثة يكون معارضة في الدليل، ويكون معارضة في العلة، فالأقسام ستة من ضرب اثنين في ثلاثة . واعلم أن أنواع المعارضة في العلة والمعارضة في الدليل سنوضحها إن شاء الله إيضاحًا تاما في التطبيق على القادح المسمى في الأصول بالمعارضة (١) ، ولذلك سنختصر القول فيهما هنا، وبالمثال يتضح معنى ما ذكرناه .
Know that mu'arada according to the practitioners of this art differs in its divisions according to different considerations. It divides according to what it targets into two types: the first is called mu'arada in the proof, and the second is called mu'arada in the cause. And it divides according to the comparison of the opponent's proof with the arguer's proof into three types: the first is mu'arada by way of reversal, the second is mu'arada by like, and the third is mu'arada by other. And every one of these three types can be mu'arada in the proof and can be mu'arada in the cause. So the types are six, from multiplying two by three. And know that the types of mu'arada in the cause and mu'arada in the proof we will clarify, God willing, with complete clarification in the application to the defect called in the principles [of fiqh] mu'arada, and therefore we will shorten the discussion here, and by the example the meaning of what we mentioned becomes clear.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage presents the classification of mu'arada (opposition) in dialectical debate. The author states that mu'arada is divided according to two considerations. First, according to what it targets: it is either mu'arada in the proof (dalil) i.e., attacking the evidence itself, or mu'arada in the cause ('illa) i.e., the reasoning or effective cause. Second, according to the relationship between the opponent's evidence and the arguer's evidence: there are three methods. The first is qalb (reversal), where the opponent turns the arguer's own evidence against him. The second is bi'l-mithl (by like), where the opponent brings similar evidence. The third is bi'l-ghayr (by other), where the opponent brings different evidence. Crucially, each of these three methods can be applied to both types of target, resulting in six possible types (2×3). The author promises to give detailed applications later when discussing the defect called mu'arada in usul al-fiqh, so here he only summarizes. This classification provides a systematic approach for the dialectician to counter arguments.

Key Takeaway
Mu'arada is systematically classified by target (proof or cause) and method (reversal, like, or other), yielding six precise types for dialectical opposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify one concrete example of mu'arada fi al-dalil from a known usul al-fiqh debate and classify it by method (qalb, mithl, or ghayr). State your reasoning.
?
Given the claim 'The world is temporally originated because it is subject to change,' describe a mu'arada fi al-'illa bi'l-ghayr against it.

Six Types of Opposition (Mu'aradah) in Dialectical Debate
Definition and classification of mu'aradah by target and method

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six types of mu'aradah by combining two targets (daleel and illah) with three methods (qalb, mithl, ghayr).

Core Idea
Every opposition targets either the proof or the cause, using reversal, similarity, or a different proof.

In dialectical debate, not all counterarguments are the same. Scholars of adab al-bahth classify opposition (mu'aradah) along two axes: what it targets and how it is carried out. Mastering these categories equips you to strategically choose the most effective form of objection against any proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two targets of opposition
Opposition can target either the proof (daleel) itself, i.e., the logical syllogism, or the cause (illah), i.e., the underlying ratio legis. This is the first classification.
The daleel is the entire argument; the illah is often the middle term or the effective cause in the proof.
2
List the three methods of opposition
The opponent may use reversal (qalb), where he turns the proof against the proponent by proving the opposite; similarity (mithl), where he presents an analogous argument to show inconsistency; or a different proof (ghayr), where he provides an entirely separate argument.
Each method can be applied to either target.
3
Combine the two classifications
Multiplying the two targets by the three methods yields six distinct types of mu'aradah: opposition in the proof by qalb, opposition in the proof by mithl, etc.
The author explicitly says the types are six from multiplying two by three.
4
Review examples of each type in practice
In the following lesson, the example of the world's origination shows how opposition could be applied. For instance, opposing the proof by qalb might involve arguing: 'If the world is changing, then it is not originated' (contradicting the original claim).
This step prepares you for the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن المعارضة عند أهل هذا الفن تختلف تقسيماتها باختلاف الاعتبارات، فتنقسم باعتبار ما توجه إليه إلى قسمين: الأول يسمى المعارضة في الدليل والثاني يسمى المعارضة في العلة . وتنقسم باعتبار مقارنة دليل المعارض بدليل المعلل إلى ثلاثة أقسام الأول المعارضة على سبيل القلب، والثاني المعارضة بالمثل، والثالث المعارضة بالغير، وكل واحد من هذه الأنواع الثلاثة يكون معارضة في الدليل، ويكون معارضة في العلة، فالأقسام ستة من ضرب اثنين في ثلاثة . واعلم أن أنواع المعارضة في العلة والمعارضة في الدليل سنوضحها إن شاء الله إيضاحًا تاما في التطبيق على القادح المسمى في الأصول بالمعارضة (١) ، ولذلك سنختصر القول فيهما هنا، وبالمثال يتضح معنى ما ذكرناه . (1). اعلم أنه لو قال المعلل مثلاً : العالم حادث، فهذا التصديق دعوى ؛ فإن أقام الدليل على هذه الدعوى فقال: لأنه متغير، وكل متغير حادث ينتج العالم حادث فهذا دليل على هذه الدعوى، ومشتمل على مقدمتين كل واحدة منهما في ذاتها دعوى . فلو أراد المعلل أن يقيم دليلاً على صغرى دليله هذا مثلاً التي العالم (متغير) فقال : لأنه لا يخلو عن الأكوان، وكل ما لا يخلو عن الأكوان فهو متغير، ينتج هو -- أي العالم - متغير . والأكوان في اصطلاح المتكلمين هي الحركة والسكون والافتراق والاجتماع. فقد عرفت من هذا الكلام أنه ثلاث طبقات : الطبقة الأولى الدعوى الأصلية التي هي (العالم حادث) . والثانية هي دليل على هذه الدعوى الذي هو لأنه متغير، وكل متغير حادث . والثالثة إقامة الدليل على صغرى هذا الدليل التي هي العالم متغير، بأن يقول : لأنه لا يخلو عن الأكوان، وكل ما لا يخلو عنها فهو متغير .
Know that al-mu'aradah (opposition) according to the scholars of this art has varying classifications depending on the perspectives. It divides by what it targets into two types: the first is called opposition in the proof (daleel), and the second is called opposition in the cause (illah). And it divides by consideration of the opponent's proof compared to the proof of the proponent into three types: first, opposition by way of reversal (qalb); second, opposition by the like (mithl); third, opposition by different (ghayr). And each of these three types can be opposition in the proof and can be opposition in the cause; thus the types are six from multiplying two by three. Know that we will explain the types of opposition in the cause and opposition in the proof fully, God willing, in the application of the objection called in usul al-fiqh al-mu'aradah, so we will shorten our discussion here, and by example the meaning of what we have mentioned becomes clear. (1) Know that if the proponent says for example: the world is originated (al-alam hadith), then this assent (tasdiq) is a claim. If he provides proof for this claim and says: because it is changing (mutaghayyir), and everything changing is originated, resulting in the world is originated, then this is a proof for this claim, and it consists of two premises, each of which is itself a claim. So if the proponent wanted to provide a proof for the minor premise of this proof, for example that the world (is changing), and says: because it is not free from accidents (akwan), and everything that is not free from accidents is changing, resulting in it—i.e., the world—is changing. And akwan in the terminology of the mutakallimun are motion, rest, separation, and conjunction. So you know from this speech that there are three layers: the first layer is the original claim which is (the world is originated). The second is the proof for this claim, which is (because it is changing, and everything changing is originated). And the third is the proof of the minor premise of this proof, which is that the world is changing, by saying: because it is not free from accidents, and everything not free from them is changing.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage from Adab Bahth is a didactic explanation of the concept of al-mu'aradah (opposition) in dialectical reasoning. The author begins by stating that the classification of mu'aradah differs according to the perspective taken. He then gives two orthogonal classifications. The first classification is based on what the opposition targets: it can target the proof itself (mu'aradah fi al-daleel) or the cause (mu'aradah fi al-illah). The second classification is based on the method of the opposing proof relative to the original proof: reversal (qalb), similar proof (mithl), or different proof (ghayr). Each of these three methods can be applied either to the proof or to the cause, yielding six possible types. The author notes that he will provide a full explanation later when discussing the objection called al-mu'aradah in usul al-fiqh, but here he gives a concise illustration. The example is drawn from the classic kalam argument for the temporality of the world: the claim is 'the world is originated.' The proof for this claim is the syllogism: 'the world is changing, and everything changing is originated.' The minor premise 'the world is changing' is itself a claim that requires proof, which the proponent provides by another syllogism: 'the world is not free from accidents, and everything not free from accidents is changing.' The author then defines 'akwan' (accidents) as motion, rest, separation, and conjunction, as per the mutakallimun. He summarizes that there are three layers: the original claim, the first proof, and the proof of the minor premise of the first proof. This example illustrates how arguments can be layered and how opposition can target different levels. The passage emphasizes the systematic nature of dialectical engagement in the Islamic logical tradition, specifically within the context of kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Key Takeaway
Mu'aradah is systematically classified by target (proof or cause) and method (reversal, similar, different), yielding six distinct types that a dialectician must master.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Given the following argument from kalam: 'The world is temporally originated because it is changing, and everything changing is temporally originated.' Identify two possible mu'aradah types that an opponent could use: one targeting the proof and one targeting the cause, and specify the method (qalb, mithl, or ghayr) for each.
?
Task 2: In a usul al-fiqh debate, the proponent argues: 'Wine is forbidden because it is an intoxicant.' The opponent uses reversal (qalb) and says: 'If intoxicants are forbidden, then vinegar is also forbidden because it can become intoxicating.' Is this a valid mu'aradah? Identify which target and method are used, and assess whether it succeeds.

Layered Arguments in Kalam: The Example of the World's Origination
Layered example of world's origination argument as illustration of mu'aradah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a layered kalam argument by identifying the original claim, the first proof, and the proof of the minor premise, and recognize how opposition can target each layer.

Core Idea
Proofs in kalam are often layered; each premise can itself be a claim requiring proof, opening multiple points for opposition.

The author illustrates the classification of mu'aradah with a concrete example from kalam: the argument for the temporality of the world. He shows three layers of argumentation, demonstrating how opposition can target each layer. This example is fundamental because it reveals the structure of complex proofs in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original claim (first layer)
The author starts with the claim 'the world is originated' (al-alam hadith). This is the primary thesis that the proponent aims to prove.
This claim is also called the matlub or mudda'a.
2
Find the first proof (second layer)
The proponent provides a syllogism: 'The world is changing, and everything changing is originated; therefore, the world is originated.' This is the proof for the original claim.
This proof consists of two premises: the minor premise (world is changing) and the major premise (everything changing is originated).
3
Extract the proof of the minor premise (third layer)
The proponent then proves the minor premise 'the world is changing' with another syllogism: 'The world is not free from accidents, and everything not free from accidents is changing; therefore, the world is changing.'
The term 'akwan' (accidents) is defined as motion, rest, separation, and conjunction.
4
Recognize how opposition can target any layer
An opponent can object to the original claim, to the first proof (e.g., deny the major premise), or to the second proof (e.g., deny that accidents imply change). Each of these would be a different type of mu'aradah depending on the target and method.
This layering is typical in kalam and usul al-fiqh, where each step of reasoning can be challenged.

The Two Main Types of Mu'arada in Dialectic
Author defines mu'arada in the proof vs. mu'arada in the premise with an example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mu'arada fi al-dalil and mu'arada fi al-'illa and identify the condition for legitimate mu'arada against a premise.

Core Idea
Mu'arada attacks either the original claim or a proven premise; unproven premises may only be rejected.

In Islamic dialectic, mu'arada is a structured counter-argument. This lesson introduces its two primary types based on target: the original claim (mu'arada fi al-dalil) or a proven premise (mu'arada fi al-'illa). A key condition is that mu'arada against a premise is only allowed if that premise has been argued for; otherwise, only rejection (man') is permitted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original claim (mudda'a)
In the example, the original claim is 'the world is originated' (العالم حادث). This is the proposition the arguer sets out to prove.
2
Recognize the three-layer argument structure
The author presents three layers: original claim, proof of original claim (using 'changing' as middle term), and proof of the minor premise of that proof (using 'akwan'). This nesting is common in dialectic.
The layers: دعوى أصلية → دليل على الدعوى → دليل على صغرى الدليل.
3
Distinguish target of mu'arada
If the opponent attacks the original claim directly by proving its contradictory, it is mu'arada fi al-dalil. If he attacks the minor premise of the original proof (after it is proven), it is mu'arada fi al-'illa.
Mu'arada fi al-dalil is also called mu'arada fi al-mad'uwa or mu'arada fi al-hukm.
4
Check the condition
Ensure the premise being attacked has been proven. If not, only man' (rejection) is allowed; mu'arada would be ghasb (usurpation).
Established earlier in the text: المعارضة لا توجه إلى إحدى مقدمات الدليل إلا إذا كانت تلك المقدمة قد استدل عليها.

فلو أراد المعلل أن يقيم دليلاً على صغرى دليله هذا مثلاً التي العالم (متغير) فقال : لأنه لا يخلو عن الأكوان، وكل ما لا يخلو عن الأكوان فهو متغير، ينتج هو ـ أي العالم - متغير . والأكوان في اصطلاح المتكلمين هي الحركة والسكون والافتراق والاجتماع فقد عرفت من هذا الكلام أنه ثلاث طبقات : الطبقة الأولى الدعوى الأصلية التي هي (العالم حادث) . والثانية هي متغير حادث . الدليل على هذه الدعوى الذي هو لأنه متغير، وكل والثالثة إقامة الدليل على صغرى هذا الدليل التي هي العالم) متغير، بأن يقول : لأنه لا يخلو عن الأكوان، وكل ما لا يخلو عنها فهو متغير . فلو أقام السائل الدليل على إبطال الدعوى الأصلية، وهي هنا العالم (حادث، فأثبت بدليله نقيضها أو مساوي نقيضها، أو أخص من نقيضها، سُمي ذلك معارضة في الدليل؛ لأنه عارض دليل إثباتها بدليل إبطالها، وتسمى أيضًا معارضة في المدعى، ومعارضة في الحكم . وإذا أقام السائل الدليل على إبطال المقدمة الصغرى من دليل الدعوى الأصلية التي هي في المثال المذكور العالم متغير)، وكان ذلك بعد إقامة المعلل الدليل على صحتها بقوله : لأنه لا يخلو عن الأكوان إلخ. ، فأثبت بدليله نقيض تلك الصغرى، أو ما يساويه، أو أخص منه بعد الاستدلال عليها، فإن ذلك يسمى معارضة في العلة، ويقال له أيضًا : معارضة في المقدمة . والحاصل أن المعارضة إن وُجهت إلى الدعوى الأصلية المدلل عليها فهي المعارضة في الدليل، وإن وجهت إلى إحدى مقدمات دليل الدعوى الأصلية فهي المعارضة في العله . وقد عرفت مما مر المعارضة لا توجه إلى إحدى مقدمات الدليل إلا إذا كانت تلك المقدمة قد استدل عليها؛ لأنها إن لم تكن قد استدل عليها لا يتوجه إليها إلا المنع وتعد معارضتها حينئذ غصبا كما تقدم إيضاحه (۱) . ، وقد علمت مما مر أن كل واحد من هذين القسمين ينقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام وهي : المعارضة على سبيل القلب والمعارضة بالمثل، والمعارضة بالغير، وسنوضح إن شاء الله - تعالى - المعارضة على سبيل القلب وغيرها أمثلة متعددة فقهية في تطبيق المعارضة على القادح المعروف في الأصول بالمعارضة (۲) ، ولذلك سنختصر الكلام عليها هنا مع . أما النوع الأول الذي هو المعارضة على سبيل القلب فهو معارضة دليل المعلل بعين دليله، وإيضاحه أن يقول له : دليلك هذا ينتج نقيض دعواك، فهو حجة عليك لا لك .
If the arguer wanted to adduce a proof for the minor premise of his proof, e.g., that the world is changing, and he said: because it does not lack accidents (akwan), and everything that does not lack accidents is changing, then the conclusion is that it—i.e., the world—is changing. And 'akwan' in the terminology of the theologians are motion, rest, separation, and conjunction. You know from this speech that there are three layers: the first layer is the original claim, which is 'the world is originated.' The second is 'everything changing is originated.' The proof for this claim is 'because it is changing, and everything changing is originated.' The third is adducing the proof for the minor premise of this proof, which is 'the world is changing,' by saying: because it does not lack accidents, and everything that does not lack them is changing. So if the opponent adduces a proof to invalidate the original claim—here, 'the world is originated'—and thereby proves by his proof its contradictory, or its equivalent, or something more specific than its contradictory, that is called 'counter-argument in the proof' (mu'arada fi al-dalil) because he countered the proof of its affirmation with a proof of its negation. It is also called 'counter-argument in the claim' and 'counter-argument in the ruling.' And if the opponent adduces a proof to invalidate the minor premise of the proof of the original claim—which in the mentioned example is 'the world is changing'—and that after the arguer adduced proof for its soundness by saying 'because it does not lack accidents, etc.,' and thereby proves by his proof the contradictory of that minor premise, or its equivalent, or something more specific, after having adduced proof for it, then that is called 'counter-argument in the cause' (mu'arada fi al-'illa), and it is also called 'counter-argument in the premise.' The upshot is that counter-argument, if directed to the original claim being proved, is counter-argument in the proof; and if directed to one of the premises of the proof of the original claim, it is counter-argument in the cause. And you have understood from what has passed that counter-argument is not directed to one of the premises of the proof unless that premise has been proven; because if it has not been proven, only rejection (man') is directed to it, and its counter-argument would then be considered usurpation (ghasb) as explained previously. And you have learned from what has passed that each of these two divisions is divided into three parts: counter-argument by way of reversal (qalb), counter-argument by likeness (mithl), and counter-argument by another (ghayr). And we will, if Allah Almighty wills, clarify counter-argument by way of reversal and others with multiple juristic examples in applying counter-argument to the well-known defect (qadih) in usul called 'mu'arada,' and therefore we will shorten the discussion here. As for the first type, which is counter-argument by way of reversal, it is countering the arguer's proof with his own proof. Its clarification is that one says to him: this proof of yours yields the contradictory of your claim, so it is an argument against you, not for you.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage from Adab al-Bahth defines and classifies mu'arada, a key dialectical technique in Islamic disputation. The author first illustrates an argument structure: the original claim 'the world is originated' is supported by a proof whose minor premise 'the world is changing' itself requires proof. The proof for that minor premise uses the concept of 'akwan' (motion, rest, separation, conjunction) as defined by theologians. The author then identifies two distinct targets for mu'arada. If the opponent attacks the original claim directly by proving its contradictory or equivalent, this is called 'mu'arada fi al-dalil' (counter-argument in the proof). If instead he attacks the minor premise of the original proof—but only after that premise has itself been proven—this is 'mu'arada fi al-'illa' (counter-argument in the cause). The author stresses a crucial condition: mu'arada against a premise is only legitimate if that premise has been proven; otherwise, the opponent may only use rejection (man'), and attempting mu'arada would be 'ghasb' (usurpation). This prevents frivolous objections. Finally, each of these two types further divides into three subtypes: mu'arada by reversal (qalb), by likeness (mithl), and by another (ghayr). The passage ends by promising juristic examples later and briefly defines qalb: the opponent uses the arguer's own proof to derive the opposite conclusion, making the proof a counter-argument against the arguer.

Key Terms
معارضة
معارضة في الدليل
معارضة في العلة
قلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example given, if the opponent tries to refute the premise 'the world is changing' without the arguer having proven it, what is the correct dialectical response?
A) Mu'arada fi al-'illa
B) Mu'arada fi al-dalil
C) Man' (rejection) only
D) Qalb (reversal)

Key Takeaway
Mu'arada must target a claim that has been argued for; unargued premises can only be rejected.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a debate on the world's origination, the opponent attacks the minor premise 'the world is changing' but the arguer has not yet proven it. Is this a legitimate mu'arada? If not, what should be the opponent's response? (Answer: Not legitimate; the opponent may only issue man' (rejection).)
?
Construct a hypothetical mu'arada fi al-dalil against the claim 'The world is originated' using a different proof from the one in the passage.

Dialectic -- Jadal
intermediate

Subtypes of Mu'arada: Qalb (Reversal)
Author defines qalb as a subtype of mu'arada and illustrates it with a general description

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define qalb (reversal) and distinguish it from other subtypes of mu'arada.

Core Idea
Qalb uses the arguer's own proof to derive the contradictory of his claim, turning his argument against him.

Beyond the two main types, mu'arada has three subtypes: qalb, mithl, and ghayr. This lesson focuses on qalb, where the opponent takes the arguer's proof and shows it leads to the opposite conclusion. This is a powerful dialectical move that requires careful handling.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two main types of mu'arada
Mu'arada fi al-dalil attacks the original claim; mu'arada fi al-'illa attacks a proven premise. These are the two overall categories.
2
Understand qalb (reversal)
Qalb is a subtype where the opponent uses the very same proof as the arguer to show that it yields the contradictory of the arguer's claim.
The author says: 'دليلك هذا ينتج نقيض دعواك' (This proof of yours yields the contradictory of your claim).
3
Identify the requirement for qalb
For qalb to be valid, the arguer's proof must be sound but its logical conclusion, when properly analyzed, contradicts his claim. The opponent must show the entailment.
Qalb is not a fallacy if correctly applied; it shows inconsistency in the arguer's position.
4
Distinguish qalb from mithl and ghayr
Mithl (likeness) uses a similar case or analogy to counter the argument. Ghayr (another) uses a different proof altogether. Qalb is unique in using the arguer's own proof.
These three subtypes apply to each of the two main types.

Mu'āraḍa bi'l-Qalb: Turning an Opponent's Argument Against Them
Author defines mu'āraḍa bi'l-qalb and illustrates with kalām and fiqh examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a valid mu'āraḍa bi'l-qalb and apply it to dialectical examples from Kalām and Fiqh.

Core Idea
Opposition by reversal turns the opponent's own argument against them by changing the perspective on the evidence.

In dialectical debate, one powerful technique is to take the opponent's own argument and show that it actually supports the opposite conclusion. This is called mu'āraḍa bi'l-qalb (opposition by reversal). The passage defines its logical conditions and illustrates with two classic examples: a Kalām debate about seeing Allah and a Fiqh dispute about wiping the head in ablution. Understanding this technique sharpens your ability to dissect arguments and find weaknesses in apparently strong proofs.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
Read the opening lines: 'It is called opposition by reversal because his argument is turned against him...' Note that the same argument is used but with a different interpretation.
2
Identify the logical conditions
The passage states that the two arguments must be identical in form (shakl) and mood (ḍarb). For categorical syllogisms, they must share the same middle term; for conditional, the same repeated part.
This ensures the reversal is a direct refutation using the opponent's own structure.
3
Analyze the Kalām example
Identify the Mu'tazili's argument: 'Vision of Allah is impermissible because it is negated by the verse...' Then see how the opponent reverses it by interpreting the verse as only negating encompassing perception.
Key concept: different mulāḥaẓa (consideration) yields opposite conclusion.
4
Analyze the Fiqh example
Al-Shafi'i argues minimal wiping suffices. The Hanafi counters that wiping is a pillar, so it requires more. Al-Shafi'i then reverses the Hanafi's own pillar argument to show a quarter is insufficient.
Notice how the reversal refutes the Hanafi's claim without introducing new premises.
5
Extract the key insight
A single argument can yield two contradictory conclusions when viewed from different aspects (mulāḥaẓāt). This is not a logical flaw but a feature of dialectical engagement.
The passage emphasizes: 'a single argument does not yield contradictory conclusions from one aspect, but may from different aspects.'

وسميت معارضة بالقلب لأنه قُلب عليه دليله بعينه حجة عليه لا له ومعلوم أن ذلك يلزمه اتحاد الدليلين دليل المعلل، ودليل المعارض، شكلاً وضربًا مع اتحادهما في الحد الوسط إن كانا اقترانيين، واتحادهما وضعا ورفعا مع اتحادهما في الجزء المكرر إن كانا استثنائيين . ومثل له بعضهم بأن يقول المعلل المعتزلي المانع رؤية الله - تعالى - : رؤية الله غير جائزة عقلاً ، ثم يقيم الدليل على ذلك في زعمه فيقول : لأنها منفية بقوله - تعالى -: ﴿ لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَرُ وَهُوَ يُدْرِكُ الْأَبْصَرُ ﴾ [الأنعام ۱۰۳] ، وكل ما كان كذلك فليس بجائز عقلاً، في زعمه : هي ليست بجائزة عقلا . ينتج فيقول السائل المعارض : رؤية الله جائزة عقلاً؛ لأنها منفية بقوله - تعالى -: ﴿ لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَرُ ، وكل ماكان كذلك فهو جائز عقلاً، ينتج : فهي جائزة عقلاً . فقد عارضه بنفس دليله وأثبت به نقيض دعواه . ولا يخفى أن لكل من السائل والمعلل ملاحظة في الدليل غير ملاحظة الآخر، فباختلاف ملاحظة السائل لملاحظة المعلل أمكنه أن يقلب عليه دليله ويستحيل قلب دليـله عليـه لا له مع اتحاد ملاحظتهما ؛ لأن الدليل الواحد لا ينتج النقيضين من جهة واحدة كما لا يخفى، لكنه قد ينتج نتيجتين متناقضتين باعتبارين مختلفين . وإيضاحه في المثال المذكور أن المعتزلي لاحظ أن قوله : تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَرُ مقتض لنفي رؤية الأبصار له مطلقا، ولكن خصمه الذي قلب عليه دليله لاحظ في الآية ملاحظة أخرى، وهي أن الآية إنما نفت الإدراك المشعر بالإحاطة فهي تدل على أن مطلق الرؤية بدون الإحاطة جائز عقلاً . ومن أمثلته الفقهية الآتية قول الشافعي: إنّ مسح الرأس في الوضوء يكفي فيه أقل ما يطلق عليه اسم المسح ، ولو كان الممسوح شعراتٍ قليلة من الرأس. ثم يقيم الدليل على ذلك فيقول : لأنه مسح ، وكل مسح يكفي فيه أقل ما يطلق عليه اسم المسح . فيعارضه الحنفي القائل بأن أقل ما يجزيء مسحه الوضوء الربع، فيقول الحنفي المعارض : مسح الرأس ركن من أركان الوضوء، وكل ركن من أركان الوضوء لا يكفي فيه أقل ما يطلق عليه ،اسم كغسل الوجه واليدين إلى المرفقين والرجلين إلى الكعبين، فلا يكفي في شيء من ذلك غسل أقل ما يطلق عليه اسم الغسل . مسح من الرأس في فيقلب الشافعي عليه دليله على سبيل المعارضة بالقلب فيقول : الرأس ركن من أركان الوضوء وكل ركن من أركان الوضوء لا يكفي فيه الربع كالوجه واليدين إلى المرفقين والرجلين إلى الكعبين ؛ فإنه لا يكفي في شيء من ذلك غسل الربع . فتراه قلب عليه دليله وأبطل دعواه أن الربع يجزىء دون أقل ما يطلق عليه الاسم، بعين دليله .
It is called opposition by reversal because his argument is turned against him as a proof against him, not for him. It is known that this requires the two arguments—the argument of the proponent and the argument of the opponent—to be identical in form (shakl) and mood (ḍarb), with their identity in the middle term if they are categorical, and their identity in placement (waḍ') and removal (raf') with their identity in the repeated part if they are conditional. Some have illustrated it by saying: the Mu'tazili proponent who denies the vision of Allah—Exalted is He—says: 'The vision of Allah is rationally impermissible,' then he establishes the argument for that in his claim, saying: 'Because it is negated by His saying—Exalted is He—{ "Eyes do not perceive Him, but He perceives eyes" } [Al-An'ām 6:103], and everything that is like that is rationally impermissible,' in his claim: 'It is not rationally permissible.' So it concludes. Then the opponent says: 'The vision of Allah is rationally permissible, because it is negated by His saying—Exalted is He—"Eyes do not perceive Him," and everything that is like that is rationally permissible,' so it concludes: 'It is rationally permissible.' So he opposed him with his very own argument and established the opposite of his claim. It is not hidden that each of the opponent and the proponent has a consideration (mulāḥaẓa) of the argument different from the other's consideration. So by the difference of the opponent's consideration from the proponent's consideration, he was able to reverse his argument against him, and it is impossible to reverse his argument against him without it being for him if their consideration were identical; because a single argument does not yield contradictory conclusions from one aspect, as is not hidden. But it may yield two contradictory conclusions according to different aspects. Its clarification in the mentioned example is that the Mu'tazili considered that His saying "Eyes do not perceive Him" necessitates the negation of the vision of eyes for Him absolutely, but his opponent who reversed his argument against him considered another consideration in the verse, which is that the verse only negates perception (idrāk) which implies encompassment (iḥāṭa), so it indicates that absolute vision without encompassment is rationally permissible. Among its fiqh examples is the following: al-Shafi'i says: 'Wiping the head in ablution suffices with the least amount to which the name "wiping" applies, even if the wiped part is a few hairs of the head.' Then he establishes the argument for that, saying: 'Because it is wiping, and every wiping suffices with the least amount to which the name "wiping" applies.' Then the Hanafi who says that the least that suffices for wiping in ablution is a quarter opposes him. The Hanafi opponent says: 'Wiping the head is a pillar (rukn) of the pillars of ablution, and every pillar of the pillars of ablution does not suffice with the least amount to which its name applies, like washing the face and the hands up to the elbows and the feet up to the ankles, so in none of that does washing the least amount to which the name "washing" applies suffice.' Then al-Shafi'i reverses his argument against him by way of opposition by reversal, saying: 'The head is a pillar of the pillars of ablution, and every pillar of the pillars of ablution does not suffice with a quarter, like the face and the hands up to the elbows and the feet up to the ankles; so in none of that does washing a quarter suffice.' So you see he reversed his argument against him and invalidated his claim that a quarter suffices without the least amount to which the name applies, with his very own argument.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage defines the dialectical technique known as mu'āraḍa bi'l-qalb (opposition by reversal). The author begins by explaining the condition for such a reversal: the two arguments must be identical in logical form (shakl) and mood (ḍarb). If they are categorical (iqtirānī) syllogisms, they must share the same middle term; if they are conditional (istithnā'ī) syllogisms, they must share the same placement and removal of the repeated part. Next, the author provides a Kalām example: a Mu'tazili argues that seeing Allah is rationally impermissible, citing the verse 'Eyes do not perceive Him' as a premise. The opponent reverses the argument by interpreting the verse differently: the verse only negates encompassing perception, not vision per se, thus concluding that vision is permissible. The key is that each party has a different 'consideration' (mulāḥaẓa) of the same evidence. The author then gives a fiqh example: al-Shafi'i argues that wiping the head in wuḍū' suffices with the minimal amount called 'wiping'. The Hanafi opponent counters that wiping is a pillar of ablution, and pillars require more than a minimal amount (like washing the face). Al-Shafi'i then reverses the Hanafi's own argument: since wiping the head is a pillar, it cannot be satisfied by a quarter (the Hanafi's own position), thus undermining the Hanafi's claim. The passage emphasizes that a single argument can yield contradictory conclusions when viewed from different perspectives.

Key Terms
المعارضة بالقلب
الحد الوسط
ملاحظة

Checkpoint

A) The two arguments must be from different scholars.
B) The two arguments must be identical in form and mood, and share the same middle term (or repeated part).
C) The opponent must use a different piece of evidence.
D) The proponent must admit defeat.

Key Takeaway
Mu'āraḍa bi'l-qalb is a powerful dialectical tool that exploits the difference in interpretation (mulāḥaẓa) to turn the opponent's own evidence against them without introducing new premises.

Explain the different mulāḥaẓas.

Defining Mu'āraḍa bi'l-Mithl: Opposition by Likeness in Dialectic
Author defines the second type of opposition, mu'āraḍa bi'l-mithl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish mu'āraḍa bi'l-mithl from qalb by identifying the difference in matter (premises) while preserving the form.

Core Idea
In mu'āraḍa bi'l-mithl, the opponent mirrors the form but replaces the premises.

In dialectical argumentation, a key skill is to counter an opponent's argument without simply denying its conclusion. Mu'āraḍa bi'l-mithl is one such refined technique: you construct a parallel argument that shares the same logical structure but uses different premises, thereby challenging the proponent's reasoning without rejecting its form. This lesson unpacks the definition and contrasts it with the related technique of qalb.

Guided Path
1
Locate the key terms 'ṣūra' and 'mādda'
Read the Arabic passage and note the words الصورة (form) and المادة (matter). The distinction between these is central to the definition.
These terms are stable across Mantiq texts.
2
Identify the condition for mu'āraḍa bi'l-mithl
The passage states that the opponent's argument must agree with the proponent's in form (ṣūra) while differing in matter (mādda). Form here includes figure (shakl) and mood (ḍarb).
The text is cut off but gives an example of both being from the first figure.
3
Contrast with qalb (opposition by reversal)
In qalb, the same matter (premises) is used but with a different perspective (e.g., converting a universal affirmative to a particular affirmative). In mithl, the matter is different entirely.
This distinction is standard in jadal literature.
4
Apply to a kalam example
Suppose a proponent argues: 'The world is temporally originated; everything temporally originated has a cause; therefore the world has a cause.' Using mithl, an opponent might argue: 'The soul is temporally originated; everything temporally originated has a cause; therefore the soul has a cause.' The form (first figure, Barbara) is identical, but the minor premise differs.
This forces the proponent to address the new analogy.
5
Verify structural parallelism
Check that both arguments share the same figure and mood. If they do, the opponent has successfully performed mu'āraḍa bi'l-mithl.
The validity of the opponent's argument is not automatically assumed; it merely challenges the proponent.

Source Text

As for the second type: it is opposition by likeness. Its rule is that the argument of the opponent agrees with the argument of the proponent in form (ṣūra) while differing in matter (mādda). That is, both arguments are from one figure (shakl), such as both being from the first figure together with...

Commentary
This passage introduces the second type of opposition in dialectical argumentation: mu'āraḍa bi'l-mithl (opposition by likeness). The author defines its condition: the opponent's argument must share the same logical form (ṣūra) as the proponent's but have different content (mādda). An example is given: both arguments are from the same syllogistic figure (e.g., the first figure), but the text is cut off. The key distinction from the first type (qalb) is that here the opponent does not use the same premises but constructs a parallel argument with the same syllogistic structure.

Mu'arada bi al-Qalb (Inversion Objection): Fiqh Example from Wudu
Author illustrates mu'arada bi al-qalb with a fiqh example from wudu' debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply mu'arada bi al-qalb in a juristic debate, using the opponent's own premises to refute his conclusion.

Core Idea
Mu'arada bi al-qalb defeats the opponent with his own argument, turning his premises against him.

In dialectical debate (jadal), one of the most powerful refutations is to invert the opponent's argument, using his own premises to derive the opposite conclusion. This technique, known as mu'arada bi al-qalb (inversion objection), is illustrated here with a classic fiqh debate between al-Shafi'i and the Hanafi school regarding the minimum amount of head wiping in wudu. By understanding this example, you will see how logical structure can be leveraged to counter a case without introducing new evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original claim
Al-Shafi'i argues that wiping the head suffices with the least amount called wiping (even a few hairs). His proof: 'it is wiping, and every wiping suffices with the least.'
This is a categorical syllogism: minor premise 'this act is wiping', major premise 'every wiping suffices with the least'.
2
Identify the Hanafi objection
The Hanafi objects that wiping the head is a pillar (rukn), and every pillar requires more than the least – like washing the face, which must cover the entire area. His premise: 'every pillar does not suffice with the least amount called the name.'
The Hanafi uses the same logical form but with a different major premise.
3
See the inversion
Al-Shafi'i inverts the Hanafi's argument: he accepts the Hanafi's major premise that every pillar requires more than the least, but applies it to the Hanafi's own claim that a quarter suffices. He argues: 'The head is a pillar, and every pillar does not suffice with a quarter (like the face). Therefore, wiping does not suffice with a quarter.'
This uses the same major premise but derives the opposite conclusion (the quarter is insufficient) instead of supporting it.
4
Check the logical structure
Notice that al-Shafi'i does not introduce new premises; he merely substitutes the middle term. The Hanafi's argument had the form: 'All pillars are X; the head is a pillar; therefore the head is X.' Al-Shafi'i uses the same form but with the Hanafi's own major premise to reach a different, contradictory conclusion.
The inversion works because the opponent's major premise is general enough to be applied to both sides.
5
Assess the outcome
The Hanafi's claim that a quarter suffices is refuted by his own reasoning. He cannot object without undermining his own position, as any objection would also invalidate his original premise. Thus, al-Shafi'i wins the debate by qalb.
This illustrates the dialectical power of mu'arada bi al-qalb: it forces the opponent into a contradiction using his own argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الذي قلب عليه دليله لاحظ في الآية ملاحظة أخرى، وهي أن الآية إنما نفت الإدراك المشعر بالإحاطة فهي تدل على أن مطلق الرؤية بدون الإحاطة جائز عقلاً . ومن أمثلته الفقهية الآتية قول الشافعي: إنّ مسح الرأس في الوضوء يكفي فيه أقل ما يطلق عليه اسم المسح ، ولو كان الممسوح شعراتٍ قليلة من الرأس. ثم يقيم الدليل على ذلك فيقول : لأنه مسح ، وكل مسح يكفي فيه أقل ما يطلق عليه اسم المسح . فيعارضه الحنفي القائل بأن أقل ما يجزيء مسحه الوضوء الربع، فيقول الحنفي المعارض : مسح الرأس ركن من أركان الوضوء، وكل ركن من أركان الوضوء لا يكفي فيه أقل ما يطلق عليه ،اسم كغسل الوجه واليدين إلى المرفقين والرجلين إلى الكعبين، فلا يكفي في شيء من ذلك غسل أقل ما يطلق عليه اسم الغسل . مسح من الرأس في فيقلب الشافعي عليه دليله على سبيل المعارضة بالقلب فيقول : الرأس ركن من أركان الوضوء وكل ركن من أركان الوضوء لا يكفي فيه الربع كالوجه واليدين إلى المرفقين والرجلين إلى الكعبين؛ فإنه لا يكفي في شيء من ذلك غسل الربع . فتراه قلب عليه دليله وأبطل دعواه أن الربع يجزىء دون أقل ما يطلق عليه الاسم، بعين دليله .
The one who inverted his argument observed another observation in the verse, namely that the verse only negates perception that implies encompassing, so it indicates that absolute vision without encompassing is rationally permissible. One of its jurisprudential examples is the statement of al-Shafi'i: 'Wiping the head in ablution is sufficient with the least amount that is called wiping, even if the wiped part is a few hairs of the head.' Then he establishes the proof for that, saying: 'Because it is wiping, and every wiping is sufficient with the least amount that is called wiping.' The Hanafi who says that the least sufficient part for wiping in ablution is a quarter opposes him. The opposing Hanafi says: 'Wiping the head is a pillar of the pillars of ablution, and every pillar of the pillars of ablution does not suffice with the least amount that is called [the name], like washing the face and hands up to the elbows and feet up to the ankles; washing the least amount called washing does not suffice for any of that.' [Wiping of the head in] Then al-Shafi'i inverts his argument against him by way of inversion objection, saying: 'The head is a pillar of the pillars of ablution, and every pillar of the pillars of ablution does not suffice with a quarter, like the face, hands up to the elbows, and feet up to the ankles; washing a quarter does not suffice for any of that.' So you see he inverted his argument against him and nullified his claim that a quarter suffices instead of the least amount called the name, with his very own argument.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage explains the first type of objection, mu'arada bi al-qalb (inversion objection), using a detailed fiqh example. The author begins by citing a kalam example about vision of God, then moves to a jurisprudential example from al-Shafi'i and Hanafi debate on wiping the head in wudu. Al-Shafi'i first argues that wiping suffices with the least amount called wiping, using a categorical syllogism: this is wiping, and every wiping suffices with the least amount. The Hanafi objects by arguing that wiping the head is a pillar, and every pillar requires more than the least, citing other pillars like washing the face. Then al-Shafi'i inverts the Hanafi's own argument: he takes the Hanafi's premise that everything that is a pillar does not suffice with the least, and applies it to the quarter claim, showing that if a quarter is insufficient for other pillars, it should also be insufficient for wiping. Thus, by using the Hanafi's own major premise, al-Shafi'i defeats the Hanafi's claim. This illustrates the essence of qalb: turning the opponent's argument against him.

Key Takeaway
Mu'arada bi al-qalb is a dialectical technique that defeats the opponent by turning his own logical premises against him, as shown in the wudu wiping debate.

Two Types of Mu'arada: Parallel and Different-Form Objections
Author defines two types of mu'arada with a combined example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish mu'arada bi al-mithl from mu'arada bi al-ghayr in a jadal exchange and identify the syllogistic forms involved.

Core Idea
Objections in jadal are classified by whether they mirror the proponent's form or change it.

In dialectical debates, not all objections are the same. The science of jadal classifies objections by their logical relationship to the proponent's proof. This lesson introduces two key types: mu'arada bi al-mithl, where the objector's proof shares the same syllogistic form as the proponent's, and mu'arada bi al-ghayr, where the forms differ. Understanding this classification allows you to navigate and construct objections strategically.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule for mu'arada bi al-mithl
The rule states that the objector's proof agrees with the proponent's in syllogistic form (e.g., both are from the first figure) but differs in material content (e.g., different middle term or premises).
Form includes figure and type (conjunctive or exceptive).
2
Identify the rule for mu'arada bi al-ghayr
Here the objector's proof differs in syllogistic form from the proponent's—different figure or different type (e.g., one conjunctive, one exceptive).
The difference can be in figure (first vs. second) or in the kind of qiyas.
3
Analyze the combined example
In the wudu debate, the proponent (Maliki/Shafi'i/Hanbali) uses a categorical syllogism (first figure). The Hanafi objector uses a conditional exceptive syllogism (modus tollens). Since the forms differ, this is mu'arada bi al-ghayr. Then the proponent counters with another conditional exceptive syllogism of the same type (modus tollens), making the counter-objection mu'arada bi al-mithl.
Notice the shift: proponent's first proof is iqtirani; objector's is istithna'i; proponent's reply is also istithna'i.
4
Check the classification
Confirm that in mu'arada bi al-mithl, the objector's proof matches the proponent's in form (e.g., both are exceptive conditionals), whereas in mu'arada bi al-ghayr, the forms differ.

Source Text

وأما النوع الثاني : وهو المعارضة بالمثل فضابطه أن يتحد دليل المعارض مع دليل المعلل في الصورة مع الاختلاف في المادة، وذلك بأن يكون الدليلان من شكل واحد ككونهما معا من الشكل الأول مع اختلافهما في الحد الوسط ،وغيره كالمقدمتين الصغرى والكبرى، وكأن يكون الدليلان معا من الشرطي المتصل المستثنى فيه نقيض التالي فيهما معا مثلاً . وأما النوع الثالث : وهو المعارضة بالغير فضابطه أن تختلف صورة دليل المعارض وصورة دليل المعلل كأن يكون دليل أحدهما من الشكل الأول ودليل الآخر من الشكل الثاني والثالث مثلاً، وكأن يكون دليل أحدهما اقترانيا ودليل الآخر استثنائيا . ومثالهما معا بتقديم المعارضة بالغير ثم المعارضة بالمثل أن يقول المعلَّل المشترط للنية في الوضوء كالمالكي والشافعي والحنبلي: النية شرط في الوضوء ثم يقيم الدليل على ذلك فيقول : لأن الوضوء طهارة هي قربة غير معقولة المعنى ، وكل ما كان كذلك تجب فيه النية ، ينتج : الوضوء تجب فيه النية . فيقول السائل كالحنفي : لو كانت الطهارة التي هي الوضوء تشترط فيها النية لكانت النية تشترط في طهارة الخبث التي هي إزالة النجاسة عن البدن والثوب مثلاً ، لكنها لا تشترط في طهارة الخبث إجماعا، ينتج : فهي لا تشترط في طهارة الحدث؛ لما قدمنا أن استثناء من نقيض التالي ينتج نقيض المقدم، فدليل المعلل من القياس الاقتراني، ودليل المعارض من القياس الاستثنائي، فهذه معارضة بالغير . فيعارض المعلل الأول دليل المعارض بقياس استثنائي من نوعه فيقول : لو كانت طهارة الحدث كطهارة الخبث لكان موجَبُها في محل موجبها، لكن موجَبَها ليس في محل موجبها، ينتج : فهي ليست
As for the second type: it is the parallel objection (mu'arada bi al-mithl). Its rule is that the proof of the objector and the proof of the proponent agree in form with difference in matter. That is, the two proofs are from one figure, for example both together from the first figure with their difference in the middle term, and other than that, like the minor and major premises. And [it also applies] if both proofs are from the conditional conjunctive [syllogism] in which the negation of the consequent is excepted in both, for example. As for the third type: it is the different-form objection (mu'arada bi al-ghayr). Its rule is that the form of the objector's proof and the form of the proponent's proof differ, such that the proof of one is from the first figure and the proof of the other is from the second or third figure, for example, or that the proof of one is conjunctive (iqtirani) and the proof of the other is exceptive (istithna'i). An example of both together, presenting mu'arada bi al-ghayr first and then mu'arada bi al-mithl, is that the proponent who stipulates intention in ablution — like the Maliki, Shafi'i, and Hanbali — says: 'Intention is a condition in ablution,' then he establishes the proof for that, saying: 'Because ablution is a purification that is an act of worship whose meaning is not rationally understood, and everything that is such requires intention.' This yields: 'Ablution requires intention.' Then the questioner, like the Hanafi, says: 'If purification that is ablution requires intention, then intention would be required in purification from impurity (taharat al-khabath), which is the removal of filth from the body or garment, for example. But it is not required in purification from impurity by consensus.' This yields: 'Thus it is not required in purification from ritual impurity (taharat al-hadath).' Because as we have stated, the exception of the negation of the consequent yields the negation of the antecedent. So the proponent's proof is a conjunctive syllogism, and the objector's proof is an exceptive syllogism — this is mu'arada bi al-ghayr. Then the first proponent objects to the objector's proof with an exceptive syllogism of its type, saying: 'If purification from ritual impurity were like purification from impurity, then its obligation would be in its place, but its obligation is not in its place.' This yields: 'It is not [like purification from impurity].'
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage defines two further types of objection in jadal: mu'arada bi al-mithl (parallel objection) and mu'arada bi al-ghayr (different-form objection). The author first gives the rule for each. For mu'arada bi al-mithl, the objector's and proponent's proofs share the same syllogistic form (figure) but differ in the middle term or premises; they are materially different but formally identical. For mu'arada bi al-ghayr, the two proofs differ in form, e.g., one is from the first figure and the other from the second, or one is a conjunctive syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) and the other is exceptive (qiyas istithna'i). Then the author provides a combined example from the debate on intention in wudu. The proponent (Maliki/Shafi'i/Hanbali) argues using a categorical syllogism: ablution is an unintelligible act of worship, and every such act requires intention, so ablution requires intention. The Hanafi objector uses a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i muttasil): if ablution requires intention, then purification from impurity would also require intention, but the latter does not require intention by consensus; therefore, ablution does not require intention. Since the forms differ (categorical vs. exceptive), this is mu'arada bi al-ghayr. Then the proponent counters with an exceptive syllogism of the same type (mu'arada bi al-mithl): if ritual purification were like impurity purification, then its obligation (the obligation of intention) would reside in the same place (i.e., be required in the same way), but it does not; therefore, they are not alike. Both arguments are exceptive, thus parallel in form.

Key Terms
المعارضة بالمثل
المعارضة بالغير
القياس الاقتراني
القياس الاستثنائي

Checkpoint

A) Conjunctive (iqtirani) categorical syllogism
B) Exceptive (istithna'i) conditional syllogism
C) A syllogism from the first figure
D) A disjunctive syllogism

Key Takeaway
Mu'arada bi al-mithl preserves the syllogistic form while changing matter, whereas mu'arada bi al-ghayr alters the form entirely.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Identify whether the following is mu'arada bi al-mithl or bi al-ghayr: Proponent: 'The world is originated because it changes.' Objector: 'If the world were originated, it would have a creator; but it does not have a creator, so it is not originated.'

Mu'arada bi al-Ghayr: Opposing by Changing Logical Form
Author defines the third type of objection (mu'arada bi'l-ghayr) and gives a fiqh example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify mu'arada bi al-ghayr in a jadal text and explain how the opponent's syllogistic form differs from the proponent's.

Core Idea
Mu'arada bi al-ghayr strikes at the form, not the content, of an opponent's proof.

In jadal, an objection (mu'arada) can target either the premise or the logical structure of an argument. The third type, mu'arada bi al-ghayr, does the latter: the opponent challenges the proponent by producing a proof that differs in figure or in type (categorical vs. conditional). This lesson unpacks the rule and a fiqh example involving intention in wudu.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent's claim and proof
Read the passage to find the proponent (a Maliki/Shafi'i/Hanbali) who claims that intention is required in wudu. They offer a categorical syllogism: 'Wudu is an act of worship whose meaning is not rationally comprehensible; everything like that requires intention; therefore wudu requires intention.' Note the structure: it is a standard Barbara syllogism (first figure).
2
Diagram the proponent's syllogism
Extract the premises and conclusion: Major premise: 'Every act of worship whose meaning is not rationally comprehensible requires intention.' Minor premise: 'Wudu is such an act.' Conclusion: 'Wudu requires intention.' This is a categorical syllogism (qiyas iqtirani).
3
Identify the opponent's objection and proof
The opponent (a Hanafi) offers a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i). They state: 'If wudu required intention, then removing impurity (taharat al-khabath) would also require intention. But removing impurity does not require intention by consensus. Therefore, wudu does not require intention.' This is modus tollens: denying the consequent.
The opponent uses a different syllogistic type (conditional) and reaches a contradictory conclusion.
4
Compare the forms of the two proofs
The proponent used a categorical syllogism (first figure). The opponent used a conditional syllogism. According to the author, this difference in form—categorical vs. conditional—is precisely what defines mu'arada bi al-ghayr.
Had the opponent used the same form (e.g., another categorical with different premises), it would be mu'arada bi al-mithl.
5
Confirm it is mu'arada bi al-ghayr
The passage explicitly states: 'So the proponent's proof is a categorical syllogism, and the opponent's proof is a conditional syllogism, and this is mu'arada bi al-ghayr.' Verify that the forms differ (categorical vs. conditional) and that the opponent's conclusion directly opposes the proponent's claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النوع الثالث : وهو المعارضة بالغير فضابطه أن تختلف صورة دليل المعارض وصورة دليل المعلل كأن يكون دليل أحدهما من الشكل الأول ودليل الآخر من الشكل الثاني والثالث مثلاً، وكأن يكون دليل أحدهما اقترانيا ودليل الآخر استثنائيا . ومثالهما معا بتقديم المعارضة بالغير ثم المعارضة بالمثل أن يقول المعلَّل المشترط للنية في الوضوء كالمالكي والشافعي والحنبلي: النية شرط في الوضوء ثم يقيم الدليل على ذلك فيقول : لأن الوضوء طهارة هي قربة غير معقولة المعنى ، وكل ما كان كذلك تجب فيه النية ، ينتج : الوضوء تجب فيه النية . • فيقول السائل كالحنفي : لو كانت الطهارة التي هي الوضوء تشترط فيها النية لكانت النية تشترط في طهارة الخبث التي هي إزالة النجاسة عن البدن والثوب مثلاً ، لكنها لا تشترط في طهارة الخبث إجماعا، ينتج : فهي لا تشترط في طهارة الحدث؛ لما قدمنا أن استثناء من نقيض التالي ينتج نقيض المقدم، فدليل المعلل من القياس الاقتراني، ودليل المعارض من القياس الاستثنائي، فهذه معارضة بالغير .
As for the third type: it is al-mu'arada bi al-ghayr. Its rule is that the form of the opponent's proof differs from the form of the proponent's proof; for example, that the proof of one of them is from the first figure and the proof of the other is from the second or third figure, or that the proof of one is categorical (iqtirani) and the proof of the other is conditional (istithna'i). And an example of both together, presenting first mu'arada bi al-ghayr then mu'arada bi al-mithl, is that the proponent who stipulates intention (niyya) in wudu—such as the Maliki, Shafi'i, and Hanbali—says: Intention is a condition in wudu. Then he establishes proof for that by saying: Because wudu is a purification that is an act of worship whose meaning is not rationally comprehensible, and everything that is like that requires intention; the conclusion: wudu requires intention. Then the opponent, such as a Hanafi, says: If the purification which is wudu required intention, then intention would be required in the purification of impurity (taharat al-khabath), which is removing filth from the body or garment, for example, but it is not required in the purification of impurity by consensus; the conclusion: so it is not required in the purification of ritual impurity (taharat al-hadath). Because we have stated that the exclusion of the negation of the consequent yields the negation of the antecedent. So the proponent's proof is a categorical syllogism, and the opponent's proof is a conditional syllogism, and this is mu'arada bi al-ghayr.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage defines the third type of objection in dialectic: mu'arada bi al-ghayr (opposition by difference in form). The author first gives the rule: the opponent's proof differs in form from the proponent's proof, for example in figure (first vs. second/third) or in type (categorical vs. conditional). Then he provides a concrete fiqh example. The proponent (a Maliki, Shafi'i, or Hanbali) argues that intention is required for wudu using a categorical syllogism: 'Wudu is an act of worship whose meaning is not rationally comprehensible, and everything like that requires intention.' The opponent (a Hanafi) responds with a conditional syllogism: 'If wudu required intention, then removing impurity would also require intention, but it does not; therefore wudu does not require intention.' This is modus tollens: denying the consequent. The author notes that the proponent's proof is categorical and the opponent's is conditional, hence this is mu'arada bi al-ghayr. Note that the opponent's argument directly contradicts the proponent's conclusion, showing how a difference in syllogistic form still constitutes valid opposition.

C) The opponent must use a categorical syllogism.
D) The opponent must use a conditional syllogism.

Key Takeaway
Mu'arada bi al-ghayr occurs when the opponent's proof differs in figure or type from the proponent's, and it is a distinct method of objection in Islamic dialectic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Mu'arada bi al-Mithl: Responding with the Same Syllogistic Form
Author presents the proponent's counter-response to mu'arada bi'l-ghayr and identifies it as mu'arada bi'l-mithl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a mu'arada bi al-mithl and construct a valid conditional syllogism to rebut an opponent's objection in a dialectical exchange.

Core Idea
Mu'arada bi al-mithl defeats the opponent by using their own logical form against them.

In a dialectical debate, the proponent must address the opponent's objection without changing the logical structure. In this passage, the proponent retaliates with the same type of conditional syllogism—mu'arada bi al-mithl. By analyzing the locus of cause and effect in purification rules, he shows that ritual impurity is unlike material impurity, thus invalidating the opponent's analogy. You will learn to recognize this powerful rebuttal technique and apply it to your own reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim under attack
The opponent's objection analogized ritual impurity (hadath) to material impurity (khabath). The proponent must refute this analogy.
The opponent's argument was presented in the previous lesson.
2
Parse the conditional syllogism
The proponent constructs a conditional: 'If hadath were like khabath, then the effect (wudu) would be in the locus of the cause (anus).' Then he denies the consequent: 'But the effect is not in that locus.' Conclusion: The antecedent is false.
This is modus tollens – a valid form of conditional argument.
3
Understand the cause-effect distinction
The author clarifies that 'mujab' (effect) is the required act (washing/wudu) and 'mujib' (cause) is the source of impurity (contact or wind). For khabath, the effect washing is at the same spot as the cause. For hadath, wudu is not at the cause's locus.
The text emphasizes the difference in Arabic between passive (mujab) and active (mujib) participles.
4
Recognize mu'arada bi al-mithl
The proponent uses the same logical form (conditional syllogism) as the opponent's original objection. This is mu'arada bi al-mithl – opposing with a mirror argument.
The author explicitly labels this as an example of mu'arada bi al-mithl.
5
Extract the dialectical lesson
In jadal, when an opponent uses a particular syllogism, the proponent may respond with a syllogism of the same type (here, conditional) to neutralize the objection without conceding the logical ground.
This preserves the debate's coherence and avoids changing the logical framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيعارض المعلل الأول دليل المعارض بقياس استثنائي من نوعه فيقول : لو كانت طهارة الحدث كطهارة الخبث لكان موجَبُها في محل موجبها، لكن موجَبَها ليس في محل موجبها، ينتج : فهي ليست كطهارة الخبث وكونها ليست مثلها مبطل لدليل المعارض؛ لأن مبناه على تماثلهما . ومعنى كون موجبها - بصيغه اسم المفعول ـ في محل موجبها ـ باسم الفاعل - أن موجب طهارة الخبث في محل موجبها ؛ لأن الشيء الذي أوجبها هو التلبس بالخبث الذي هو النجاسة، وموجبها هو الغسل ؛ لأن الخبث أوجب الغسل، ولا يلزم إلا غسل عين المحل الذي فيه النجاسة فإن كانت النجاسة في اليد لم يلزم إلا غسل اليد، ولا يلزم غسل الرجل، فموجَبها - بالفتح ـ وهو الغسل في محل موجبها - بالكسر - وهو الخبث ، بخلاف طهارة الحدث؛ فإن موجبها - بالفتح - ليس في محل موجبها - بالكسر ، فلو خرجت ريح منه فخروجها موجب للوضوء، فالوضوء موجب - باسم المفعول الذي ربما عبرنا عنه بالفتح ، وخروج الريح موجب للوضوء - باسم الفاعل الذي ربما عبرنا عنه بالكسر ، ومحل الموجب ـ بالكسر ـ الدبر ؛ لأنه هو الذي خرجت منه الريح، والموجب الذي هو الوضوء ليس في محل الدبر، بل هو في الوجه واليدين إلى المرفقين ومسح الرأس والرجلين إلى الكعبين، فكون موجب طهارة الخبث في محل موجبها يدل على أنها معقولة المعنى، فلا تُشترط لها النية، كون موجب طهارة الحدث في غير محل موجبها يدل على أنها ليست معقولة المعنى، فتشترط لها النية . ومعارضة الدليل الثاني بالدليل الثالث في هذه الأمثلة مثال للمعارضة بالمثل ؛ لأن الدليل في كل منهما قياس استثنائى متصل،
So the first proponent opposes the opponent's proof with a conditional syllogism of its type, saying: If the purification of ritual impurity (taharat al-hadath) were like the purification of material impurity (taharat al-khabath), then its effect would be in its cause's locus, but its effect is not in its cause's locus; the conclusion: it is not like the purification of impurity, and its not being like it invalidates the opponent's proof because it is based on their similarity. And the meaning of 'its effect' (with the passive participle form) being 'in its cause's locus' (with the active participle) is that the effect of purification of impurity is in its cause's locus: because the thing that caused it (the cause) is the contact with impurity (al-najasah), and its effect is the washing (ghusl) because impurity necessitates washing, and only washing of the exact locus that contains the impurity is required; if the impurity is on the hand, only washing the hand is required, not the foot. So its effect (with fatha, passive) i.e. the washing is in its cause's locus (with kasra, active) i.e. the impurity. Unlike purification of ritual impurity: its effect (passive) is not in its cause's locus (active). For example, if wind passes from a person, its emission necessitates wudu, so wudu is the effect (passive, with fatha) and the emission of wind is the cause (active, with kasra). The locus of the cause (with kasra) is the anus because it is from where the wind exited, but the effect which is wudu is not in the locus of the anus; rather it is on the face, hands to the elbows, wiping the head, and feet to the ankles. So the fact that the effect of purification of impurity is in its cause's locus indicates that it is rationally comprehensible, so intention is not required for it. The fact that the effect of purification of ritual impurity is not in its cause's locus indicates that it is not rationally comprehensible, so intention is required for it. And the opposition of the second proof with the third proof in these examples is an example of mu'arada bi al-mithl, because the proof in each of them is a continuous conditional syllogism.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage continues the dialectical exchange from the previous lesson. The proponent (the mu'allil) now refutes the opponent's objection by constructing a conditional syllogism of the same type (istithna'i). The proponent argues: If ritual impurity (hadath) were like material impurity (khabath) in being rationally comprehensible, then the effect (the required purification act) would be located in the same place as the cause. But in ritual impurity, the effect (wudu) is not located at the site of the cause (the passage of wind). Therefore, ritual impurity is not like material impurity. The author explains the distinction between the two types of purification by analyzing the terms 'effect' (mujab, passive participle) and 'cause' (mujib, active participle). For material impurity, the cause is contact with filth, and the effect is washing the particular spot. The effect is in the same locus as the cause. For ritual impurity, the cause is the emission of wind, and the effect is wudu, which is performed on the face, hands, etc., not at the anus. Hence, material impurity is 'ma'qulat al-ma'na' (its wisdom is understandable), so intention is not required for its removal. Ritual impurity is not 'ma'qulat al-ma'na', so intention is required for wudu. This response uses mu'arada bi al-mithl because the proponent uses the same type of conditional syllogism as the opponent. The author explicitly states that opposing the second proof (the opponent's) with the third proof (the proponent's) is an example of mu'arada bi al-mithl, since both are continuous conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i muttasil).

موجَب
موجِب

Key Takeaway
Mu'arada bi al-mithl is a powerful dialectical tool that responds to an objection using the same logical form, here a conditional syllogism, to defeat the opponent's analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Construct a mu'arada bi al-mithl using a conditional syllogism to defend wiping over socks by drawing an analogy to another accepted exception (e.g., wiping a bandage).

Using Cause-Effect Locality to Determine Rationally Understandable Rulings
Author explains the distinction between khabath and hadath using cause-effect locality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the cause-effect locality test to determine whether a fiqh ruling is ma'qulat al-ma'na (rationally understandable) and thus whether intention (niyya) is required.

Core Idea
The place of the effect relative to the cause reveals whether a ruling is rational or devotional.

In this passage, the author uses the example of physical impurity (khabath) versus ritual impurity (hadath) to illustrate a key logical principle: if the effect of a ruling (the required action) occurs in the same physical location as its cause (the impurity or event), then the ruling is rationally understandable (ma'qulat al-ma'na), and intention is not required. If the effect occurs elsewhere, the ruling is purely devotional, requiring intention. This principle is a tool for distinguishing rulings that can be rationally extended via qiyas from those that are ta'abbudi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the cause (mūjib) and effect (mūjab) in each case
For khabath, the cause is the presence of impurity on a body part, and the effect is washing that same part. For hadath, the cause is the exiting of wind from the anus, and the effect is wudu performed on the face, hands, head, and feet. Note: The author uses grammatical terms: mūjib (active participle) for cause, mūjab (passive participle) for effect.
2
Compare the location of the effect to the location of the cause
For khabath, the washing is on the same spot as the impurity (e.g., hand). For hadath, the wudu is not on the anus but on other body parts.
3
Apply the rule: if effect is in the place of cause, the ruling is ma'qulat al-ma'na and intention is not required
Since khabath's effect is localized, it makes rational sense—no need for specific intention. Hadath's effect is not localized, indicating it is a devotional act requiring intention.
4
Generalize this test for other fiqh rulings
This test helps jurists determine which rulings can be extended by analogy (qiyas) and which are restricted to their specific textual basis. It is a standard tool in usul al-fiqh for classifying ahkam.

كطهارة الخبث وكونها ليست مثلها مبطل لدليل المعارض؛ لأن مبناه على تماثلهما .
ومعنى كون موجبها - بصيغه اسم المفعول ـ في محل موجبها ـ باسم الفاعل - أن موجب طهارة الخبث في محل موجبها ؛ لأن الشيء الذي أوجبها هو التلبس بالخبث الذي هو النجاسة، وموجبها هو الغسل ؛ لأن الخبث أوجب الغسل، ولا يلزم إلا غسل عين المحل الذي فيه النجاسة فإن كانت النجاسة في اليد لم يلزم إلا غسل اليد، ولا يلزم غسل الرجل، فموجَبها - بالفتح ـ وهو الغسل في محل موجبها - بالكسر - وهو الخبث ، بخلاف طهارة الحدث؛ فإن موجبها - بالفتح - ليس في محل موجبها - بالكسر ، فلو خرجت ريح منه فخروجها موجب للوضوء، فالوضوء موجب - باسم المفعول الذي ربما عبرنا عنه بالفتح ، وخروج الريح موجب للوضوء - باسم الفاعل الذي ربما عبرنا عنه بالكسر ، ومحل الموجب ـ بالكسر ـ الدبر ؛ لأنه هو الذي خرجت منه الريح، والموجب الذي هو الوضوء ليس في محل الدبر، بل هو في الوجه واليدين إلى المرفقين ومسح الرأس والرجلين إلى الكعبين، فكون موجب طهارة الخبث في محل موجبها يدل على أنها معقولة المعنى، فلا تُشترط لها النية، كون موجب طهارة الحدث في غير محل موجبها يدل على أنها ليست معقولة المعنى، فتشترط لها النية .
ومعارضة الدليل الثاني بالدليل الثالث في هذه الأمثلة مثال للمعارضة بالمثل ؛ لأن الدليل في كل منهما قياس استثنائي متصل، استُثني فيه نقيض التالي فأنتج نقيض المقدم كما ترى .
ومن أمثلة المعارضة بالمثل ما قدمناه من قول معتقد قدم العالم : العالم أثر القديم، وكل ماهو أثر القديم فهو قديم، ينتج في زعمه : العالم قديم . فيعارضه السائل بالمثل فيقول : العالم متغير، وكل متغير حادث، ينتج : العالم حادث فكل واحد من دليل المعلل ودليل المعارض اقتراني من الشكل الأول، وإن اختلف فيهما الحد الوسط والمقدمتان كما أوضحناه .
Like purification from physical impurity, and that it is not like it invalidates the proof of the opponent; because its basis is on their similarity. And the meaning of its active participle's effect being located in the place of its cause is that the effect of purification from physical impurity is in the place of its cause; because the thing that necessitated it is being beset with physical impurity which is filth, and its necessitating thing is washing; because physical impurity necessitates washing, and nothing is necessitated except washing the very place that contains the filth. So if the filth is on the hand, only washing the hand is necessitated, and washing the foot is not necessitated. So its effect (with fatha) which is washing is in the place of its cause (with kasra) which is physical impurity, unlike purification from ritual impurity; for its effect (with fatha) is not in the place of its cause (with kasra). For if wind exits from him, its exiting necessitates ablution, so ablution is the effect (by the passive voice which we sometimes express with fatha), and the exiting of wind necessitates ablution (by the active participle which we sometimes express with kasra), and the place of the cause (with kasra) is the anus; because it is that from which the wind exited, and the effect which is ablution is not in the place of the anus, but is on the face and hands to the elbows, and wiping the head and feet to the ankles. So the fact that the effect of purification from physical impurity is in the place of its cause indicates that it is rationally understandable, so intention is not required for it. The fact that the effect of purification from ritual impurity is not in the place of its cause indicates that it is not rationally understandable, so intention is required for it. And countering the second proof with the third proof in these examples is an example of counterargument by likeness; because the proof in each of them is a conditional conjunctive syllogism, in which the contrary of the consequent was excepted, and it produced the contrary of the antecedent, as you see. And among examples of counterargument by likeness is what we previously presented from the statement of the one who believes in the eternity of the world: 'The world is an effect of the Eternal, and everything that is an effect of the Eternal is eternal,' producing in his claim: 'The world is eternal.' The questioner counters him by likeness, saying: 'The world is changing, and everything changing is originated,' producing: 'The world is originated.' So each of the proof of the proponent and the proof of the opponent is a categorical syllogism of the first figure, even if the middle term and the two premises differ in them, as we have explained.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage explains the concept of mu'āraḍa bi al-mithl (counterargument by likeness) using two concrete examples. It begins by discussing the difference between ṭahārat al-khabath (physical impurity) and ṭahārat al-ḥadath (ritual impurity) to illustrate a logical point about whether the ruling is 'ma'qūlat al-ma'nā' (rationally understandable). For khabath, the effect (ghusl/washing) is located in the same physical place as the cause (the impurity), so it is rational—hence no intention (niyya) is required. For ḥadath, the effect (wuḍū') is not located in the place of the cause (the anus), so it is not rational—hence intention is required. This distinction is used to show that the second proof and third proof in the examples are subject to mu'āraḍa bi al-mithl. The author then explicitly states that countering the second proof with the third is an example of mu'āraḍa bi al-mithl because both proofs are conditional conjunctive syllogisms (qiyās istithnā'ī muttaṣil) where the contradictory of the consequent is excepted, yielding the contradictory of the antecedent. Finally, another example is given from the debate on the eternity of the world: the proponent argues 'The world is an effect of the Eternal, and every effect of the Eternal is eternal; therefore the world is eternal.' The opponent counters with a parallel syllogism: 'The world is changing, and everything changing is originated; therefore the world is originated.' Both are categorical syllogisms of the first figure (al-shakl al-awwal), but with different middle terms and premises. This demonstrates how mu'āraḍa bi al-mithl works: the opponent presents an argument of identical logical form but with different content, leading to a contradictory conclusion, thereby refuting the original claim.

Dialectic -- Jadal
