Constructing a Parallel Syllogism: Mu'arada bi al-Mithl in Action
Author explains mu'arada bi al-mithl using the world syllogism example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a mu'arada bi al-mithl (counterargument by likeness) by producing a parallel first-figure categorical syllogism with a contradictory conclusion.

Core Idea
Mu'arada bi al-mithl defeats an argument by mirroring its form with different premises.

The passage provides a classic example of mu'arada bi al-mithl from the kalam debate on the eternity of the world. The opponent of the argument that the world is eternal counters with a parallel syllogism: the world is changing, and everything changing is originated, so the world is originated. Both syllogisms are first-figure categorical (AAA-1), but they lead to contradictory conclusions. This demonstrates that mu'arada bi al-mithl does not attack the premises directly but shows that the same reasoning form can be used to prove the opposite, thereby undermining the original claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent's original syllogism
The proponent argues: The world is an effect of the Eternal (minor premise). Everything that is an effect of the Eternal is eternal (major premise). Therefore, the world is eternal (conclusion). This is a first-figure categorical syllogism with middle term 'effect of the Eternal'.
2
Identify the opponent's counter-syllogism (mu'arada bi al-mithl)
The opponent counters: The world is changing (minor premise). Everything changing is originated (major premise). Therefore, the world is originated (conclusion). This is also first figure, with middle term 'changing'.
3
Compare the logical forms
Both syllogisms are in the mood Barbara (AAA-1). The difference is in the terms: the middle term and the predicates differ. The opponent's conclusion ('originated') directly contradicts the proponent's conclusion ('eternal').
4
Understand the purpose of mu'arada bi al-mithl
The opponent does not deny the proponent's premises but offers a parallel argument that appears equally valid, thereby showing that the original argument is not decisive. This forces the proponent to provide further justification or refute the counter-syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Dialectic -- Jadal

Three Answers of the Prover to the Objection
Three responses to a counter-argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three responses (man', naqd, and new proof) that a prover can use when his proof is countered by an objection.

Core Idea
A prover has three weapons: denial, refutation, or a fresh proof.

In a dialectical exchange (jadal), after the objector (sa'il) raises a mu'arada against the prover's (mu'allil) proof, the prover is not defenseless. The author of this passage enumerates exactly three legitimate responses. Understanding these options is essential for anyone engaged in structured theological or legal debates, as taught in the adab al-bahth tradition.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the dialectical situation
Understand that the objector has already raised a counter-argument (mu'arada) against the prover's proof. The prover must now choose a response from the three options.
2
Identify the first answer: man' (denial)
The prover can deny a premise of the objector's proof that the objector has not yet proven. This shifts the burden of proof back to the objector, who must then provide evidence for that premise.
This is standard jadal procedure: one does not accept an unsubstantiated premise.
3
Identify the second answer: naqd ijmali (general refutation)
The prover can invalidate the objector's proof by showing either that the conclusion does not follow (takhalluf) or that the premises lead to an impossibility (muhal). This is called a 'general refutation'.
This attacks the logical connection itself, not just individual premises.
4
Identify the third answer: new proof
The prover can abandon the proof that was objected to and produce a completely different proof for the same claim. The author considers this acceptable and potentially beneficial.
The new proof may be stronger than the objection, or at least strengthen the original proof when combined.
5
Compare strategic value
Consider when each response is most appropriate. Man' is useful when the objector relies on unproven premises. Naqd is effective when the objector's reasoning is flawed. A new proof is a fresh start and may overcome a strong objection.
The author hints that a new proof, even if individually weaker than the objection, can combine with the original to outweigh it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أجوبة المعلل عن المعارضة اعلم أن السائل إذا عارض دليل المعلل بنوع من أنواع المعارضة التي بينا فللمعلل أن يجيب عن تلك المعارضة بواحد من ثلاثة أجوبة : الأول منها : أن يمنع بعض مقدمات دليل المعارض التي لم يقم عليها المعارض دليلاً، بأن يطلب منه إثبات المقدمة بالدليل الدال على صحتها على نحو ما قدمنا في المنع الثاني : أن يبطل دليل المعارض بالنقض، بأن يقول : إن مدلوله متخلف عنه، أو إنه مستلزم للمحال . وقد قدمنا أنه يقال له النقض الإجمالي . والثالث : أن يثبت دعواه بدليل آخر غير الدليل الذي أورد السائل عليه المعارضة والأظهر أن هذا الأخير مقبول، وأنه يفيد المعلل ؛ [ الجواز ] أن يكون هذا الدليل الأخير الذي أقامه المعلل بعد المعارضة أقوى من دليل المعارض، ولأن دليله الأخير يقوي دليله الأول، وقد يكون مجموعهما أقوى من دليل المعارض، وإن كان دليل المعارض أقوى من كل واحد منهما بانفراده :
Chapter on the answers of the prover to the objection. Know that if the objector counters the proof of the prover with one of the types of objection we have explained, the prover may answer that objection with one of three answers: First: to deny some premises of the objection's proof for which the objector has not provided a proof, by asking him to prove the premise with a proof indicating its correctness, as we introduced in [the chapter on] denial. Second: to invalidate the objection's proof by refutation (naqd), by saying that its conclusion is inconsistent with it, or that it entails an impossibility. We have already mentioned that this is called the general refutation. Third: to establish his claim by another proof besides the proof against which the objector raised the objection. The most apparent is that this last one is acceptable and benefits the prover; it is possible that this later proof which the prover produced after the objection is stronger than the objection's proof, and because his latter proof strengthens his first proof, and perhaps their combination is stronger than the objection's proof, even if the objection's proof is stronger than each one of them individually.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the possible responses (ajwiba) of the mu'allil (the prover) when faced with a mu'aradah (objection). The author states that the objector (sa'il) may challenge the prover's proof with any of the previously explained types of objection. The prover has three options: First, he can deny (man') some premises of the objector's proof, provided the objector has not already proven those premises. The prover then demands that the objector provide evidence for the contested premise. This is a standard dialectical move: one does not accept an unsubstantiated premise. Second, he can invalidate the objector's proof by pointing out an inconsistency (naqd): either the conclusion does not follow (takhalluf) or the premises lead to an absurdity (muhal). This is called 'general refutation' (al-naqd al-ijmali). Third, he can produce a new separate proof for his original claim, different from the one that was objected to. The author considers this third answer acceptable, noting that the new proof may be stronger than the objector's, or at least strengthen the original proof when combined. Even if the objector's proof is individually stronger than either of the prover's proofs, their combination may overcome it. The passage ends mid-discussion (with a colon), suggesting further elaboration on the relative strength.

Key Terms
المعلل
منع
نقض إجمالي

Key Takeaway
The prover has three separate, structured responses to an objection: denial of an unproven premise (man'), general refutation (naqd ijmali), or producing a new proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following debate: The prover claims 'Every intoxicant is forbidden (haram).' The objector says 'But vaccine alcohol is not forbidden.' Which of the three responses could the prover use, and how? Write a brief scenario for each possible response.
?
In a jadal exchange, identify which response is being used: The prover says 'You have not proven that vaccine alcohol is not forbidden; I deny that premise.' This is an example of which response? (Answer: man').

Three Dialectical Answers of the Prover
Author enumerates three ways a prover can answer an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the three dialectical responses (man', naqd, and bringing a new proof) that a prover can use against an objection in a munazara exchange.

Core Idea
Denial, refutation, and a new proof – three weapons for the prover in debate.

In a dialectical exchange, the prover (المعلل) must defend his claim against objections (المعارضة). This passage from Adab al-Bahth teaches you the three legitimate moves he can make: deny an unproven premise, refute the logic of the objection, or bring an entirely new proof. Let's walk through each one with the example of the 'world is originated' debate.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context of objection
Recognize that the opponent (المعارض) has raised an objection against the prover's proof. The prover must respond without abandoning his claim.
2
Identify the first answer: Denial (المنع)
The prover denies a premise of the opponent's proof that the opponent has not yet established with evidence. He demands proof for that premise.
This shifts the burden of proof to the opponent.
3
Identify the second answer: Refutation (النقض)
The prover invalidates the opponent's proof by showing that the conclusion does not follow or that it leads to an impossibility. This is called 'general refutation'.
The source gives an example of this earlier, but the text here is incomplete.
4
Identify the third answer: New proof (دليل آخر)
The prover abandons the original proof and brings a new one for his claim. The author argues this is acceptable because the new proof may be stronger or combine with the original to outweigh the objection.
Some scholars reject this as abandoning the original proof, but the author accepts it.
5
Apply to the given example
In the example, the prover (Sunni) says the world is originated. The opponent (philosopher) says it is eternal because the Creator is eternal. The prover denies the minor premise 'the Creator's existence is a cause' and demands proof.
This illustrates man' effectively.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أجوبة المعلل عن المعارضة
اعلم أن السائل إذا عارض دليل المعلل بنوع من أنواع المعارضة التي بينا فللمعلل أن يجيب عن تلك المعارضة بواحد من ثلاثة أجوبة :
الأول منها : أن يمنع بعض مقدمات دليل المعارض التي لم يقم عليها المعارض دليلاً، بأن يطلب منه إثبات المقدمة بالدليل الدال على صحتها على نحو ما قدمنا في المنع
الثاني : : أن يبطل دليل المعارض بالنقض، بأن يقول : إن مدلوله متخلف عنه، أو إنه مستلزم للمحال . وقد قدمنا أنه يقال له النقض الإجمالي.
والثالث : أن يثبت دعواه بدليل آخر غير الدليل الذي أورد السائل عليه المعارضة والأظهر أن هذا الأخير مقبول، وأنه يفيد المعلل ؛ [ الجواز ] أن يكون هذا الدليل الأخير الذي أقامة المعلل بعد المعارضة أقوى من دليل المعارض، ولأن دليله الأخير يقوي دليله الأول، وقد يكون مجموعهما أقوى من دليل المعارض، وإن كان دليل المعارض أقوى من كل واحد منهما بانفراده :
لا تخاصم بواحد أهل بيت فضعيفان يغلبان قويا
خلافا لقوم من أهل هذا الفن منعوا هذا الوجه الأخير ؛ لأنه انتقال من حجة إلى أخرى بعد إبطال الحجة الأولى، والأول أظهر كما ذكرنا والعلم عند الله تعالى .
ومثال منع بعض مقدمات دليل المعارض أن يقول المعلل : العالم حادث. ثم يقيم الدليل على ذلك فيقول: لأنه متغير بالانعدام والاجتماع والافتراق ونحو ذلك، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو حادث، ينتج : هو ـ أي العالم ـ حادث .
فيعارضه الفلسفي الضال القائل بقدم العالم فيقول : العالم قديم . ثم يقيم الدليل على ذلك في زعمه الفاسد فيقول : لأن وجود الخالق القديم علةٌ في وجود العالم، وكل شيء علة وجوده قديمة فهو قديم، ينتج : العالم قديم .
فيجيب المعلل عن دليل المعارض هذا بمنع الصغرى فيقول : أمنع قولك (وجود الخالق القديم علة لوجود العالم)، وأقم دليلاً على ذلك . ولن يقيمه أبدًا.
ومثال إبطاله دليل المعارض بالنقض هو ما قدمنا من أن المعلل السني يقول : العالم حادث. ثم يقيم الدليل على ذلك فيقول : لأنه
Chapter on the answers of the prover to the objection.
Know that when the opponent objects to the prover's proof with some type of the objections we have mentioned, the prover can answer that objection with one of three answers:
The first of them: that he denies some premises of the opponent's proof that the opponent has not proven by evidence, by demanding from him to establish the premise with proof indicating its correctness, in the manner we have already presented under denial.
The second: that he invalidates the opponent's proof by refutation, by saying that its implication is left behind, or that it entails an impossibility. We have already said that it is called 'general refutation'.
The third: that he establishes his claim with another proof other than the proof against which the opponent brought the objection. The more apparent is that this last one is acceptable and benefits the prover; because it is possible that this last proof which the prover established after the objection is stronger than the opponent's proof, and because his last proof strengthens his first proof, and their combination may be stronger than the opponent's proof, even if the opponent's proof is stronger than each one of them individually:
'Do not argue with one of the family, for two weak ones overcome a strong one'
Contrary to a group of the people of this art who forbade this last method, because it is a transition from one proof to another after nullifying the first proof. The first is more apparent as we mentioned, and knowledge is with Allah Almighty.
An example of denying some premises of the opponent's proof is that the prover says: the world is originated. Then he establishes proof for that, saying: because it is subject to change by ceasing to exist, combination, separation, and the like, and everything that is so is originated, resulting: the world is originated.
Then the misguided philosopher who believes in the eternity of the world objects, saying: the world is eternal. Then he establishes proof for that in his corrupt opinion, saying: because the existence of the eternal Creator is a cause for the existence of the world, and everything whose cause of existence is eternal is eternal, resulting: the world is eternal.
Then the prover answers this opponent's proof by denying the minor premise, saying: I deny your statement 'the existence of the eternal Creator is a cause for the existence of the world', and provide a proof for that. He will never establish it.
An example of him invalidating the opponent's proof by refutation is what we have presented: that the Sunni prover says: the world is originated. Then he establishes proof for that, saying: because...
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical chapter explaining the three methods a prover (المعلل) can use to answer an objection (معارضة) raised by an opponent (المعارض). The author first enumerates the three answers. 

The first answer is to deny (منع) some premise of the opponent's proof that the opponent has not yet proven. The prover demands that the opponent establish that premise with a proper proof. This is similar to the earlier discussion on denial. 

The second answer is to invalidate the opponent's proof by refutation (نقض). The prover can claim that the implication (مدلول) of the opponent's proof is not actually produced (متخلف عنه), meaning the conclusion does not follow, or that the proof leads to an impossibility (مستلزم للمحال). The author notes that this is called 'general refutation' (النقض الإجمالي). 

The third answer is to provide another proof for his original claim, distinct from the proof that was objected to. The author argues that this is acceptable and beneficial to the prover, because the new proof might be stronger than the opponent's proof, or it may strengthen the first proof. The combination of the two proofs can be stronger than the opponent's proof even if each alone is weaker. The author quotes a poetic line to illustrate that two weak forces can overcome a strong one. He then mentions that some scholars of this art have rejected this third method, considering it a transition from one proof to another after the first was invalidated, but the author maintains the first view (acceptance) as more apparent. 

The author then provides an example for the first method: the prover (a Sunni theologian) claims that the world is originated (حادث) and proves it by change. The opponent (a misguided philosopher arguing for the eternity of the world) claims the world is eternal and argues that since the Creator is eternal, His effect (the world) must also be eternal. The prover denies the minor premise (that the Creator's existence is a cause for the world's existence) and demands a proof, which the opponent cannot provide. 

The example for the second method is incomplete in the text; it only begins by stating that the Sunni prover argues the world is originated, and then the text cuts off. The author refers to an earlier presentation (on page 234) of this example.

Key Terms
المعلل
المعارضة
المنع
النقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Adab Bahth, which of the following is NOT one of the three ways the prover can answer an objection?
A) Deny some premises of the opponent's proof that lack evidence.
B) Invalidate the opponent's proof by refutation (naqd).
C) Accept the objection and change his own claim.
D) Establish his claim with another proof besides the one objected to.

Key Takeaway
The prover has three legitimate responses to an objection: denial (man'), refutation (naqd), and bringing a new proof; the third is accepted by most scholars and can strengthen the overall argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a debate where the opponent objects that 'nabidh is not prohibited because it is not explicitly named in the Quran', which of the three answers would a Hanafi jurist use? Provide the specific move and a brief defence.
?
Construct your own dialectical exchange similar to the example: choose an Islamic claim (e.g., 'prayer is obligatory on every Muslim'), have an opponent raise a mu'arada, and respond using man'.

Denying a Premise in Dialectical Debate (Man')
Worked example of denying a premise in dialectical debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the dialectical technique of man' (denial of a premise) to refute a counterargument in a theological debate.

Core Idea
To refute an opponent, you don't need to attack the conclusion—just deny a key premise.

In dialectical debates (jadal/munazara), sometimes the most effective response to a counterargument is not to attack its conclusion directly, but to challenge a specific premise that supports it. This move is called man' (denial). In this lesson, you will see a classic example from Islamic theology: the Sunni theologian denies the philosopher's claim that the existence of the eternal Creator is the cause of the world's existence, thereby blocking the philosopher's argument for the world's eternity. Understanding man' sharpens your ability to dissect arguments and respond precisely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent's original argument
The theologian (mu'allil) argues the world is originated (hadith). His syllogism: the world changes; everything that changes is originated; therefore the world is originated. Understand this first before moving to the counterargument.
2
Identify the counterargument (mu'arada)
The philosopher counters with a different syllogism: the existence of the eternal Creator is a cause for the world's existence; everything whose cause is eternal is itself eternal; therefore the world is eternal. Note how this directly opposes the theologian's conclusion.
3
Locate the premise that is denied (man')
The theologian does not accept the philosopher's minor premise: 'the existence of the eternal Creator is a cause for the world's existence.' He explicitly says 'I deny your statement' (أمنع قولك). Identify precisely which proposition is being rejected.
The denial targets the causal link between the Creator and the world's existence.
4
Understand the dialectical burden
By denying the premise, the theologian shifts the burden of proof to the philosopher. The philosopher must now provide evidence for that premise. The author notes that the philosopher will never be able to establish it, highlighting the strategic advantage of man'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثال منع بعض مقدمات دليل المعارض أن يقول المعلل : العالم حادث. ثم يقيم الدليل على ذلك فيقول: لأنه متغير بالانعدام والاجتماع والافتراق ونحو ذلك، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو حادث، ينتج : هو ـ أي العالم ـ حادث . فيعارضه الفلسفي الضال القائل بقدم العالم فيقول : العالم قديم . ثم يقيم الدليل على ذلك في زعمه الفاسد فيقول : لأن وجود الخالق القديم علةٌ في وجود العالم، وكل شيء علة وجوده قديمة فهو قديم، ينتج : العالم قديم . فيجيب المعلل عن دليل المعارض هذا بمنع الصغرى فيقول : أمنع قولك (وجود الخالق القديم علة لوجود العالم)، وأقم دليلاً على ذلك . ولن يقيمه أبدًا.
Example of denying some premises of the opponent's proof: The proponent says: 'The world is originated (hadith).' Then he establishes the argument for that by saying: 'Because it changes through annihilation, conjunction, separation, and the like, and everything that is such is originated,' resulting in: 'It — i.e., the world — is originated.' Then the misguided philosopher who asserts the eternity of the world counters him by saying: 'The world is eternal.' Then he establishes the argument for that in his corrupt opinion by saying: 'Because the existence of the eternal Creator is a cause for the existence of the world, and everything whose cause of existence is eternal is itself eternal,' resulting in: 'The world is eternal.' So the proponent answers this counterargument by denying the minor premise, saying: 'I deny your statement that the existence of the eternal Creator is a cause for the existence of the world, and I will establish a proof for that.' But he will never establish it.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage provides a concrete example of the dialectical technique known as 'man'' (denial) used in response to a mu'arada (counterargument). The scenario is a classic theological debate: a Sunni theologian (al-mu'allil) argues that the world is temporally originated (hadith) because it undergoes change (tahawwul) — annihilation, conjunction, separation. This is a categorical syllogism: premise 1: the world is changing; premise 2: everything changing is originated; conclusion: the world is originated. The philosopher (al-faylasuf al-dall) counters with his own syllogism: premise 1: the existence of the eternal Creator is the cause of the world's existence; premise 2: anything whose cause is eternal is itself eternal; conclusion: the world is eternal. The theologian does not attack the conclusion directly; instead, he denies the minor premise of the philosopher's argument — he rejects the claim that the Creator's existence is the cause of the world's existence. He then asserts that he can prove this denial (i.e., show that premise is false), though the author notes wryly that he will never actually do so. This illustrates that man' is a legitimate dialectical move: you may accept your opponent's conclusion (the world is eternal? No, the theologian does not accept that, but he addresses the premise) — actually here the theologian denies the premise and does not accept the conclusion. The point is that in jadal, one can block an argument by challenging a specific premise, forcing the opponent to provide evidence for it. The example highlights the asymmetry: the theologian's denial is a challenge that the philosopher cannot easily meet.

Key Terms
منع
معارض
صغرى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) That the world changes
B) That changing things are originated
C) That the existence of the eternal Creator is a cause for the world's existence
D) That the world is eternal

Key Takeaway
Man' is a dialectical move that blocks a counterargument by denying a specific premise, shifting the burden of proof to the opponent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Construct a short debate: The proponent argues that 'the Quran is uncreated' because 'it is the speech of Allah and Allah's speech is uncreated'. The opponent counters with 'the Quran is created because it is composed of letters and sounds, and everything composed is created'. Apply man' to deny a premise of the opponent's argument.

Refuting an Opponent's Argument with a Counterexample
Author shows how to refute an opponent's argument by providing a counterexample where the same premise leads to a false conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the technique of naqd (refutation by counterexample) to invalidate an opponent's categorical syllogism in a dialectical exchange.

Core Idea
A single counterexample can dismantle an entire syllogism if it shares the same premise.

In dialectical debates, proving an opponent wrong often requires more than simply denying their conclusion. One powerful method is naqd: showing that their own premise leads to an absurd or false conclusion in another case. The passage below illustrates this by having the theologian refute the philosopher's argument for the world's eternity using everyday events as a counterexample. Let's unpack the logical structure of this move.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposing positions
The theologian claims the world is originated; the philosopher claims it is eternal.
The philosopher's position is presented as a counterargument (mu'arada) to the theologian.
2
Extract the philosopher's argument as a categorical syllogism
Major premise: Everything that is an effect of the Eternal is eternal. Minor premise: The world is an effect of the Eternal. Conclusion: The world is eternal.
The syllogism is in first figure, mood Barbara, but its major premise will be attacked.
3
Find the theologian's counterexample
Daily events (like accidents) are also effects of the Eternal but are not eternal.
The counterexample must share the same property (being an effect of the Eternal) but lack the supposed consequence (being eternal).
4
Apply the counterexample to the philosopher's syllogism
Since the major premise claims 'Every effect of the Eternal is eternal,' but daily events are counterexamples, the major premise is false. Therefore, the syllogism is unsound.
This is a refutation by counterexample (naqd). The theologian does not reject the minor premise; he undermines the major premise.
5
Understand the logical effect: the syllogism is invalidated
The argument is not just opposed; its logical foundation is shown to be false by a single clear case.
The naqd is 'overall' (ijmali) because it refutes the entire argument without dissecting each premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثال إبطاله دليل المعارض بالنقض هو ما قدمنا من أن المعلل السني يقول : العالم حادث. ثم يقيم الدليل على ذلك فيقول : لأنه متغير بالانعدام والاقتران والاجتماع ونحو ذلك، وكل ماهو كذلك فهو حادث، فالعالم حادث . فيعارضه السائل الفلسفي فيقول : العالم قديم . ثم يقيم الدليل في زعمه الباطل على ذلك فيقول : لأنه أثر القديم، وكل ماهو أثر القديم فهو قديم . فيجيب المعلل عن دليل المعارض هذا بالنقض الإجمالي فيقول : هذا الدليل منقوض بالحوادث اليومية المشاهد تجددها؛ لأنها أثر القديم وليست بقديمه، فالدليل منقوض بتخلف مدلوله عنه كما تقدم إيضاحه (۱)
And the example of his refutation of the opponent's argument by counterexample is what we previously mentioned: that the Sunni proponent says: 'The world is originated.' Then he establishes the argument for that by saying: 'Because it changes through annihilation, conjunction, union, and the like, and everything that is such is originated, so the world is originated.' Then the philosophical questioner counters him, saying: 'The world is eternal.' Then he establishes the argument in his false claim by saying: 'Because it is an effect of the Eternal, and everything that is an effect of the Eternal is eternal.' So the proponent answers this counterargument by an overall counterexample, saying: 'This argument is refuted by the daily events whose renewal is observed; because they are effects of the Eternal but are not eternal. So the argument is refuted because its implication fails to hold, as previously explained.'
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage illustrates the dialectical technique of naqd (counterexample) to refute a mu'arada. The scenario is the same: the theologian argues the world is originated due to change; the philosopher argues the world is eternal because it is an effect of the Eternal. The theologian does not deny the premise; instead, he provides a counterexample: daily events (like accidents) are also effects of the Eternal but are not eternal. Therefore, the philosopher's syllogism is invalid because its conclusion does not follow from its premises: being an effect of the Eternal does not entail being eternal, as shown by daily events. This is a classic naqd: one shows that the opponent's reasoning leads to a false conclusion (or that a case with the same premise fails to have the same property). The author notes that this technique was explained earlier. The counterexample is 'overall' (ijmali) because it is a general refutation without breaking down the premise.

Key Terms
نقض (naqd): Refutation by counterexample; showing that the opponent's argument fails because there exists a clear case where the same reasoning leads to a falsehood. [Note: In later logic, naqd can also refer to the relation of contradictories; here it is specifically a dialectical move.]
دليل المعارض (dalil al-mu'arida): The argument of the opponent in a dialectical exchange; here specifically the philosopher's proof for the eternity of the world.
أثر القديم (athar al-qadim): Effect of the Eternal; a concept used by philosophers to argue that whatever is caused by an eternal cause must itself be eternal. [Note: This is a key philosophical claim in the debate on the world's eternity.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the counterexample used in the naqd refutation against the philosopher's argument?
A) The world itself
B) The Creator's attributes
C) Daily events (accidents) that are effects of the Eternal but not eternal
D) The theologian's own argument

Key Takeaway
Naqd refutes an opponent's argument by providing a specific case where the same reasoning leads to an obviously false conclusion, thus invalidating the general claim.

Intiqal: When Can You Switch Arguments in a Debate?
Author debates the permissibility of intiqal after counterargument, provides kalam example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the dialectical move of intiqal and evaluate its permissibility when a proponent shifts arguments after a counterargument.

Core Idea
Intiqal is controversial: some forbid it, but it can be a valid move when your first argument is countered.

In dialectical theology (jadal), knowing how to respond when your argument is countered is crucial. One option is intiqal: abandoning your first argument and presenting a completely new proof. But is this allowed? This lesson examines the debate over intiqal and provides a concrete kalam example to test your understanding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions on intiqal
The passage states that a group of scholars forbade intiqal because it involves shifting from one argument to another after invalidating the first. The author favors allowing it. Note the reasoning on each side.
The author says 'al-awwal azhar' (the first view is more apparent), indicating his preference.
2
Extract the first argument and its counterargument
The Sunni proponent's first argument: The world is originated because it changes. The philosopher's counterargument: The world is eternal because it is an effect of the Eternal. Write each as a categorical syllogism.
These are two opposing claims about the same subject.
3
Find the new argument after the shift
The proponent shifts to a new proof: The world is created (based on Quran 39:62), and everything created is originated. Therefore the world is originated. This is a different foundation—revelation instead of change.
The new argument does not refute the counterargument; it offers an independent proof.
4
Analyze the logical relationship between the arguments
The first argument and the new argument are both for the same conclusion (world is originated) but rely on different premises. The counterargument directly attacks the first argument's conclusion, but the new argument sidesteps it by providing a fresh line of evidence.
This is why intiqal is controversial: it may appear as evading the counterargument.
5
Assess the author's stance and rationale
The author states 'al-awwal azhar'—the view allowing intiqal is more apparent. He does not fully elaborate why, but the example shows that intiqal can be a legitimate move when a proponent has multiple independent proofs.
In later adab al-bahth works, conditions are added to regulate intiqal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خلافا لقوم من أهل هذا الفن منعوا هذا الوجه الأخير؛ لأنه انتقال من حجة إلى أخرى بعد إبطال الحجة الأولى، والأول أظهر كما ذكرنا والعلم عند الله تعالى . ومثال إقامة المعلل دليلاً آخر بعد المعارضة أن يقول المعلل السني : العالم حادث؛ لأنه متغير بأنواع التغير التي ذكرنا، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو حادث فالعالم حادث. فيقول المعارض الفلسفي الضال: العالم قديم؛ لأنه أثر القديم وكل ماهو أثر القديم فهو قديم، فالعالم قديم . فينتقل المعلل إلى دليل آخر يثبت به حدوث العالم غير دليله الأول فيقول : كل العالم مخلوق ؛ لدخوله في عموم قوله تعالى: اللهُ خَلِقُ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ [الزمر ،٦٢] وكل مخلوق فهو حادث، ينتج : كل العالم حادث .
Contrary to a group among the practitioners of this art who forbade this last method, because it is a transition from one argument to another after invalidating the first argument. The first view is more apparent as we mentioned, and knowledge is with Allah Most High. And the example of the proponent presenting another argument after the counterargument is: The Sunni proponent says: 'The world is originated, because it changes with the kinds of change we mentioned, and everything that is such is originated, so the world is originated.' Then the misguided philosophical opponent says: 'The world is eternal, because it is an effect of the Eternal, and everything that is an effect of the Eternal is eternal, so the world is eternal.' So the proponent shifts to another argument by which he proves the world's origination other than his first argument, saying: 'All of the world is created, because it is included in the generality of His statement: "Allah has created all things" [Quran 39:62], and everything created is originated.' This yields: 'All of the world is originated.'
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage addresses a point of disagreement in dialectical method: whether it is permissible for a proponent to abandon his original argument after it has been countered (mu'arada) and present a completely new proof. Some scholars (qawm) forbid this, arguing that it is a mere shift from one argument to another after the first has been invalidated. The author, however, favors allowing it (al-awwal azhar — 'the first view is more apparent'), indicating that shifting to a new proof is acceptable. He then provides an example: the theologian's first argument for the world's origination is based on change. The philosopher counters with the effect-of-the-Eternal argument. Instead of refuting that counterargument directly, the theologian shifts to a new argument: the world is created because the Quran says so (Q39:62), and everything created is originated. This new argument is independent and does not rely on refuting the counterargument. The passage illustrates that intiqal is a valid strategy in jadal, though controversial.

Key Terms
انتقال
وجه أخير
معارضة

C) It requires the opponent's permission
D) It is only allowed in religious debates

Key Takeaway
Intiqal is a permissible dialectical move when your first argument is countered; it allows you to present an independent proof without directly refuting the counterargument.

General Refutation (Naqd Ijmaili) in Dialectical Debate
Author illustrates general refutation against philosopher's eternity argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a general refutation (naqd ijmaili) by providing a counterexample that shares the opponent's premise but contradicts the conclusion.

Core Idea
A single counterexample can refute an entire proof without dismantling its structure.

In dialectical debate, sometimes the most effective response is not a detailed analysis but a single counterexample. This is called naqd ijmaili. In this passage, the mutakallim refutes the philosopher's proof for the eternity of the world using everyday observable events as a counterexample.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosopher's argument
The philosopher argues: The world is eternal because it is an effect of the Eternal, and everything that is an effect of the Eternal is eternal. Write down the premises and conclusion clearly.
This is a categorical syllogism in the first figure (Barbara).
2
Isolate the universal premise
The key claim is 'Every effect of the Eternal is eternal.' This is the premise the mutakallim targets. Note that it claims universality without exception.
3
Find the counterexample
The mutakallim points to daily events (like accidents) that are effects of the Eternal (God) but are not eternal themselves. Verify that they satisfy the premise but not the conclusion.
A valid counterexample must be an instance of the premise that does not lead to the conclusion.
4
Understand why this refutes the proof
If a universal claim has even one counterexample, it is false. Thus the philosopher's premise is invalid, and the proof collapses. This is a general refutation (naqd ijmaili) because it does not require analyzing the proof's internal structure.
The refutation is effective even if the philosopher adjusts his argument, as long as the counterexample remains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيعارضه السائل الفلسفي فيقول : العالم قديم . ثم يقيم الدليل في زعمه الباطل على ذلك فيقول : لأنه أثر القديم، وكل ماهو أثر القديم فهو قديم . فيجيب المعلل عن دليل المعارض هذا بالنقض الإجمالي فيقول : هذا الدليل منقوض بالحوادث اليومية المشاهد تجددها؛ لأنها أثر القديم وليست بقديمه، فالدليل منقوض بتخلف مدلوله عنه كما تقدم إيضاحه
So the philosophical questioner opposes him and says: The world is eternal. Then he establishes the proof in his false claim by saying: Because it is the effect of the Eternal, and everything that is an effect of the Eternal is eternal. So the mu'allil responds to this opposing proof with a general refutation and says: This proof is refuted by the daily events that are observed to be renewed; because they are effects of the Eternal and are not eternal. So the proof is refuted by its consequent falling away from it, as its explanation has preceded.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage illustrates the method of naqd ijmaili (general refutation) in dialectical debate. The philosopher argues the world is eternal because it is an effect of an eternal cause, with the premise that every effect of the Eternal is eternal. The mutakallim refutes this by pointing to everyday observable events—like accidents—that are also effects of the Eternal but are not eternal themselves. This shows that the philosopher's universal claim fails because its conclusion does not necessarily follow from its premise; a counterexample exists where the premise holds but the conclusion does not. The mutakallim thus refutes the proof without dismantling its internal structure, simply by showing that its form permits exceptions.

Strategic Shift: Moving to a New Proof After Mu'arada
Author demonstrates a debater shifting to a new proof after the opponent's counter-argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the strategic shift (intiqaal) in a dialectical exchange and reconstruct the categorical syllogism used in the new proof.

Core Idea
When your opponent produces a counter-proof, abandon your weak proof and pivot to a stronger one.

In the art of munazara, a debater must know when to retreat from a challenged position and advance a fresh line of reasoning. This lesson uses a classic kalam debate on the world's origin to illustrate the tactic of 'moving to a new proof' (intiqaal) after a mu'arada. You will see how the Sunni mutakallim, faced with the philosopher's argument for the world's eternity, abandons his first proof and introduces a Qur'anic proof to re-establish his claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original claim and proof
The Sunni mutakallim first claims 'the world is originated' (al-‘alamu hadith). His proof: the world changes; everything that changes is originated. This is a categorical syllogism: 'The world changes; everything that changes is originated; therefore the world is originated.'
This is the argument from change (al-taghayyur), a standard kalam proof.
2
Recognize the mu'arada (counter-argument)
The philosopher counters with a contradictory proof: 'The world is eternal; because it is the effect of the Eternal; everything that is an effect of the Eternal is eternal.' This mu'arada directly opposes the original thesis with a competing syllogism.
Mu'arada is not mere denial; it provides an alternative proof for the contradictory claim.
3
Observe the shift (intiqaal)
Instead of refuting the philosopher's mu'arada, the mutakallim abandons his first argument and produces an entirely new proof. He shifts from rational argument to scriptural evidence.
This is a strategic choice: the new proof is independent and does not rely on the first argument.
4
Analyze the new proof's structure
The new proof is a categorical syllogism: 'Every world is created (due to Qur'an 39:62); everything created is originated; therefore every world is originated.' The major premise is from revelation, the minor is a general principle.
The syllogism is in the first figure (Barbara mood) and is valid.
5
Compare the two proofs
The first proof was based on change, the second on divine creation. Both conclude the same thesis, but the second avoids the philosopher's counter-argument by using a different foundation.
Intiqaal demonstrates flexibility; the debater's thesis remains consistent across proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثال إقامة المعلل دليلاً آخر بعد المعارضة أن يقول المعلل السني : العالم حادث؛ لأنه متغير بأنواع التغير التي ذكرنا، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو حادث فالعالم حادث. فيقول المعارض الفلسفي الضال: العالم قديم؛ لأنه أثر القديم وكل ماهو أثر القديم فهو قديم، فالعالم قديم . فينتقل المعلل إلى دليل آخر يثبت به حدوث العالم غير دليله الأول فيقول : كل العالم مخلوق ؛ لدخوله في عموم قوله تعالى: اللهُ خَلِقُ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ [الزمر ،٦٢] وكل مخلوق فهو حادث، ينتج : كل العالم حادث .
An example of the mu'allil establishing another proof after the opposition is that the Sunni mu'allil says: The world is originated; because it is changing by the types of change that we mentioned, and everything that is like that is originated, so the world is originated. So the misguided philosophical opponent says: The world is eternal; because it is the effect of the Eternal, and everything that is an effect of the Eternal is eternal, so the world is eternal. So the mu'allil moves to another proof by which he establishes the origination of the world other than his first proof, and says: All the world is created; due to its inclusion under the generality of His statement, may He be exalted: 'Allah is the Creator of everything' (az-Zumar 39:62), and everything created is originated, yielding: All the world is originated.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage shows a complete dialectical exchange: the mutakallim first argues for the world's origination (hadath) using the argument from change. The philosopher counters with the argument from the Eternal cause, claiming the world is eternal. Instead of refuting the counter-argument directly (as in the previous example), the mutakallim here produces an entirely new proof from revealed scripture. He cites the Qur'anic verse that Allah is the Creator of everything, and then adds that everything created is originated, concluding that the world is originated. The new proof is a categorical syllogism: 'All the world is created; everything created is originated; therefore, all the world is originated.' This illustrates the tactic of changing the line of argument ('intiqaal) when the first is met with opposition, a key skill in munazara.

Key Takeaway
In munazara, when faced with a strong mu'arada, a debater may strategically abandon the original argument and present an entirely new, independent proof (intiqaal) to uphold his thesis.

The Procedure of Istifsaar: Clarifying Ambiguous or Strange Terms
Author explains the first step in dialectical debate: clarifying ambiguous or unfamiliar terms in the claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of istifsār by identifying when a term is ambiguous (mujmal) or strange (gharīb) and executing the procedure for establishing ambiguity in a dialectical exchange.

Core Idea
In dialectic, unclear terms must be clarified before argument can proceed.

This lesson introduces the first step in a dialectical debate: checking the wording of the claim. If a term is ambiguous (having multiple meanings) or unfamiliar (strange), the listener has the right to request clarification (istifsār). The burden is on the listener to prove the ambiguity or strangeness, and the claimant must respond. We will learn how this procedure works with examples from classical debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim (da‘wa) presented
When a proposition is presented, note its wording. The claimant (mu‘allil) is responsible for the claim.
2
Scan the wording for ambiguous or strange terms
Look for words that have multiple meanings (ijmāl) or are rare (gharābah). For example, 'العين' (the eye) is ambiguous; 'الزخيخ' (fire) is strange.
3
Request clarification (istifsār) if found
If such a term exists, you (mustafsir) may ask the mu‘allil to specify the intended meaning.
4
Establish the ambiguity or strangeness
If the mu‘allil denies ambiguity, you must prove it. For ambiguity, showing at least two possible meanings suffices. For example, 'العين' can mean the seeing organ, a water spring, or currency.
5
Recognize the mu‘allil's possible responses
One response is to deny multiple probabilities (man‘ ta‘addud al-ihtimāl). Further responses are discussed in the full text.
The passage here only covers the first response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في ترتيب المناظرة في التصديق حسب ما تقدم متفرقا في هذه المذكرة؛ لأن جمعه في محل واحد بعد تفرقه يعين طالب العلم على إدراكه بسرعة. اعلم أولاً أنك إذا ألقي إليك تصديق ـ ويقال له هنا : الدعوى، والمدعى - فانظر في ألفاظه أولاً، فإن كان في بعض ألفاظه إجمال أو غرابة فلك حينئذ الاستفسار، وهو طلب تفسير اللفظ الذي فيه إجمال وغرابة، أي تعيين المراد به. ومثال ما فيه إجمال ما لو قال : تؤخذ العين بالعين؛ فإن لفظ العين فيه إجمال ؛ لصدقه على الباصرة والجارية وغيرهما. ومثال ما فيه غرابة أن يقول : ( من قتل بالزخيخ قتل به)، فالزخيخ وهو النار لفظ فيه غرابة. وعلى المستفسر إثبات الإجمال والغرابة، ويكفيه في إثبات الإجمال بيان احتمالين في اللفظ، كما لو قال: يجب أخذ العين بالعين. فقال المستفسر ما مرادك بالعين؟ فقال المعلل : ليس في كلامي إجمال حتى تستفسر عنه فيثبت المستفسر الإجمال بأن العين تُطلق على الباصرة والجارية والنقد وغير ذلك. وإذا أثبت المستفسر الاحتمال فجواب المعلل صاحب التصديق بأحد أمرين : الأول : منع تعدد الاحتمال كما لو قال المعلل : يجوز لك أن
Chapter on the order of the debate regarding assent, as previously scattered in this memorandum; because collecting it in one place after its dispersion helps the student of knowledge to grasp it quickly. Know first that when a proposition of assent is presented to you—and it is called here 'the claim' (al-da‘wa) and 'the claimed' (al-mudda‘ā)—then look at its wording first. If any of its wording contains ambiguity (ijmāl) or strangeness (gharābah), then you have the right to request clarification (istifsār), which is asking for the interpretation of the word that contains ambiguity or strangeness, i.e., specifying what is meant by it. An example of what contains ambiguity is if someone says: 'The eye is taken for the eye'; for the word 'eye' has ambiguity because it applies to the seeing organ, the running water, and others. An example of what contains strangeness is if he says: 'Whoever kills by fire (al-zakhīkh) is killed by it'; for al-zakhīkh, which is fire, is a strange word. The person requesting clarification (mustafsir) must establish the ambiguity or strangeness. It suffices for him in establishing ambiguity to show two possibilities in the word, as if someone says: 'It is obligatory to take the eye for the eye.' Then the mustafsir says: 'What do you mean by eye?' The mu‘allil says: 'There is no ambiguity in my speech for you to ask about.' Then the mustafsir establishes the ambiguity by stating that 'eye' is applied to the seeing organ, the running water, currency, and others. And when the mustafsir establishes the probability, the response of the mu‘allil, the owner of the proposition, is one of two things: the first is: denial of multiple probabilities, as if the mu‘allil says: 'You may...'
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is a procedural guide for the art of dialectic (munazara), specifically regarding propositions of assent (tasdiq). The author begins by stating that he is collecting rules that were previously scattered. He then outlines the first step: when a claim (da‘wa) is presented to you, examine its wording. If there is ambiguity (ijmāl) or strangeness (gharābah), you have the right to request clarification (istifsār). Ambiguity occurs when a word has multiple meanings, like 'ayn (eye) which can mean the physical eye, a water spring, or gold coin. Strangeness occurs when a word is unfamiliar, like zakhīkh (fire). The person requesting clarification (mustafsir) must prove the ambiguity or strangeness: for ambiguity, showing at least two possible meanings suffices. The claimant (mu‘allil) then must respond. The author mentions the first possible response: denial of multiple probabilities, which is cut off at the end of the page. The procedure ensures that the debate proceeds on clear terms.

Initial Steps in Disputation: Clarifying Ambiguity and Assessing the Origin of the Claim
Author explains the first steps in disputation: examine wording for ambiguity, request clarification, and verify the source of the claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to execute the correct sequence of steps when faced with an ambiguous or obscure term in a disputed claim, including requesting clarification and verifying whether the claim is transmitted or original.

Core Idea
In disputation, clarify terms and source before rebutting.

When a claim is presented in a debate, you cannot immediately attack it. The first duty is to ensure that the terms are clear. If a word is ambiguous (ijmal) or obscure (gharaba), you have the right to request clarification (istifsar). The claimant must then either show that the ambiguity is eliminated by context, or argue that his intended meaning is the most apparent. Only after the wording is clear must you determine whether the claimant is merely transmitting the claim from another source or asserting it himself. If transmitting without commitment, the proper demand is for him to verify the transmission. This passage establishes the foundational protocol of formal disputation.

Guided Path
1
Identify potential ambiguity or obscurity
When you hear the claim, scan its words. Are there any words that could have multiple meanings (ijmal) or are rarely used (gharaba)? Example: 'eye' in 'the eye is taken for the eye' can mean the seeing organ, a water spring, or currency. The respondent must demonstrate the multiplicity of meanings to prove ambiguity.
The passage also gives 'al-zakhikh' (fire) as an example of obscurity.
2
Request clarification (istifsar)
If you detect an ambiguous or obscure word, ask the claimant to specify what he means. The claimant (mu'allil) must respond.
3
Analyze the claimant's response to the clarification request
The claimant has two main options: (a) deny multiple possibilities by invoking a contextual indicator (qarina) that restricts the meaning, e.g., 'drinking' specifies 'eye' as water; or (b) acknowledge multiple meanings but argue that one is more apparent (azhar) and must be taken, e.g., 'lion' primarily means the animal.
If the claimant admits ambiguity/obscurity, he must explain his intended meaning.
4
Once terms are clear, assess the nature of the claim
Determine if the claimant is transmitting the claim from a book or a transmitter, or if he is originating it himself.
5
If transmitting without commitment, demand verification
If the claimant is only transmitting and does not commit to the truth of the claim, you cannot attack the claim itself; you can only challenge the transmission by demanding that he verify its authenticity.
This step prevents debating a claim that hasn't been endorsed by the speaker.

Source Text

فصل في ترتيب المناظرة في التصديق حسبما تقدم متفرقا في هذه المذكرة؛ لأن جمعه في محل واحد بعد تفرقه يعين طالب العلم على إدراكه بسرعة . اعلم أولاً أنك إذا أُلقي إليك تصديق ـ ويقال له هنا : الدعوى، والمدعى - فانظر في ألفاظه أولاً، فإن كان في بعض ألفاظه إجمال أو غرابة فلك حينئذ الاستفسار، وهو طلب تفسير اللفظ الذي فيه إجمال وغرابة، أي تعيين المراد به . ومثال مافيه إجمال ما لو قال : تؤخذ العين بالعين؛ فإن لفظ العين فيه إجمال ؛ لصدقه على الباصرة والجارية وغيرهما . ومثال مافيه غرابة أن يقول : ( من قتل بالزخيخ قتل به)، فالزخيخ وهو النار لفظ فيه غرابة . وعلى المستفسر إثبات الإجمال والغرابة، ويكفيه في إثبات الإجمال بيان احتمالين في اللفظ، كما لو قال: يجب أخذ العين بالعين. فقال المستفسر ما مرادك بالعين؟ فقال المعلل : ليس في كلامي إجمال حتى تستفسر عنه فيثبت المستفسر الإجمال بأن العين تُطلق على الباصرة والجارية والنقد وغير ذلك وإذا أثبت المستفسر الاحتمال فجواب المعلل صاحب التصديق بأحد أمرين : الأول : منع تعدد الاحتمال كما لو قال المعلل : يجوز لك أن تمنع الشرب من عينك. فيقول :المستفسر ما مرادك بالعين؟ فيقول : ليس في كلامي إجمال؛ لأنه لا يحتمل إلا معنى واحدا. فيقول المستفسر : أليس لفظ العين في كلامك مشتركا بين الباصرة والجارية وغيرهما؟ فيقول المعلل : لفظ الشرب في كلامي قرينة تعيّن أن مرادي العين الجارية لا الباصرة ولا غيرها فلا يحتمل كلامي إلا معنى واحدا هو العين الجارية . الثاني : ترجيح الاحتمال الذي قصده على غيره من الاحتمالات كأن يقول المعلل : ذلك الشبح أسد فيقول المستفسر : ما مرادك بالأسد ؟ فيقول : لا إجمال في كلامي فيقول الآخر: أليس الأسد يطلق على الحيوان المفترس وعلى الرجل الشجاع؟ فيقول الآخر : هو في الحيوان المفترس أظهرُ منه في الرجل الشجاع عند التجرد من القرائن، وحمل اللفظ على الأظهر لازم؛ لرجحانه على غيره . وإذا كان المعلل معترفًا بالغرابة أو الإجمال لزمه بيان مراده من كلامه . وإذا كان لفظ صاحب التصديق ظاهر المعنى واضح الكلمات لا إجمال فيه ولا غرابة أو كان فيه إجمال أو غرابة وبينه صاحبه حتى صار واضح المعنى لا إشكال فيه من غرابة أو إجمال = فعليه بعد هذه الخطوة أن ينظر في كلام المعلل صاحب التصديق : هل هو ناقل لذلك التصديق عن غيره من كتاب أو راو؟ أو هو آت به من تلقاء نفسه؟ وفي حال كونه ناقلاً له عن غيره : هل هو ملتزم صحته أو ليس ملتزما لها؟ فإن كان ناقلاً ولم يلتزم صحة ما نقل فليس له أن يوجه إليه شيئًا إلا المطالبة بتصحيح النقل، ويلزمه حينئذ تصحيح نقله بإثباته ذلك بطريق
Chapter on the arrangement of disputation concerning assent, as was previously scattered in this memorandum; because gathering it in one place after its dispersion helps the student of knowledge to grasp it quickly. Know first that when a statement of assent is presented to you — and it is called here the claim (al-da'wa) and the thing claimed (al-mudda'a) — then look at its words first; if some of its words contain ambiguity (ijmal) or obscurity (gharaba), then you have at that time the right to request clarification (istifsar), which is asking for an explanation of the word that has ambiguity or obscurity, i.e., specifying what is meant by it. An example of what has ambiguity: if someone says 'The eye is taken for the eye'; for the word 'eye' has ambiguity, because it applies to the seeing eye, the running water, and others. An example of what has obscurity: to say 'Whoever kills with al-zakhikh is killed by it'; al-zakhikh, which is fire, is a word of obscurity. The one requesting clarification must prove the ambiguity and obscurity, and it suffices for him in proving ambiguity to show two possibilities in the word, as if someone says: 'It is obligatory to take the eye for the eye.' Then the one requesting clarification says: 'What do you mean by eye?' The explainer says: 'There is no ambiguity in my speech for you to ask about.' Then the one requesting clarification proves the ambiguity by saying that 'eye' is applied to the seeing eye, the running water, currency, and others. When the requester has proven the possibility, the response of the explainer, the owner of the statement, is one of two things: First: denial of multiple possibilities, as if the explainer says: 'It is permissible for you to prevent drinking from your eye.' The requester says: 'What do you mean by eye?' He says: 'There is no ambiguity in my speech, because it only bears one meaning.' The requester says: 'Is not the word 'eye' in your speech shared between the seeing eye, the running water, and others?' The explainer says: 'The word 'drinking' in my speech is a contextual indicator that specifies that I mean the running water, not the seeing eye nor others; so my speech bears only one meaning, which is the running water.' Second: preferring the intended probability over other probabilities, as if the explainer says: 'That figure is a lion.' The requester says: 'What do you mean by lion?' He says: 'There is no ambiguity in my speech.' The other says: 'Is not 'lion' applied to the predatory animal and to the brave man?' The other says: 'It is more apparent in the predatory animal than in the brave man when free of contextual indicators, and it is necessary to take the word in its more apparent sense, due to its preponderance over others.' And if the explainer acknowledges the obscurity or ambiguity, he must explain his intended meaning from his speech. And if the wording of the owner of the statement is clear in meaning, unambiguous in words, with no ambiguity or obscurity, or if there was ambiguity or obscurity and its owner clarified it until it became clear in meaning with no problem from obscurity or ambiguity, then after this step he must look at the speech of the explainer, the owner of the statement: Is he transmitting that statement from someone else, from a book or a transmitter? Or is he presenting it from his own mind? And in case he is transmitting it from someone else: Is he committed to its correctness or not? If he is transmitting and has not committed to the correctness of what he transmitted, then the only thing he can be directed is to demand the correction of the transmission, and at that time he must correct his transmission by establishing it by way of...
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is a procedural guide for the initial steps in a disputation (munazara) concerning a claim of assent (tasdiq). The author begins by gathering previously scattered advice into one place for convenience. The first step is to examine the wording of the claim. If a word is ambiguous (ijmal) — having multiple possible meanings — or obscure (gharaba) — rarely used or unclear — the respondent has the right to request clarification (istifsar). Examples are given: 'eye' in 'the eye is taken for the eye' is ambiguous because it can mean the seeing organ, a water spring, or even currency. 'Al-zakhikh' (fire) is obscure. The respondent must demonstrate the ambiguity by showing at least two possible meanings. Once the possibility is shown, the claimant (mu'allil) has two options: either deny that multiple meanings are possible by invoking a contextual indicator that restricts the meaning, e.g., 'drinking' specifies 'eye' as water; or acknowledge the multiple meanings but argue that one meaning is more apparent (azhar) and must be taken as the intended one, like 'lion' primarily meaning the animal. If the claimant admits the word is obscure or ambiguous, he must explain his intended meaning. After the wording is clear, the next step is to ascertain whether the claimant is merely transmitting the claim from a book or transmitter, or if he is originating it himself. If he is a transmitter and not committed to the truth of the statement, the only valid response is to demand that he verify the transmission (tas-hih al-naql). The passage emphasizes that the process of disputation begins with ensuring that the terms are understood and that the nature of the claim (transmitted or original) is clear. The text ends mid-sentence, but the principle is established.

In a disputation, always start by clarifying terms and the nature of the claim before proceeding to argumentation.

Handling Ambiguity in Disputant's Speech
Author defines ijmal and gharaba with examples and rules for clarification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify ambiguous expressions in a dialectical exchange and determine whether a contextual clue resolves the ambiguity or the speaker must clarify.

Core Idea
Ambiguity in debate is resolved by a clue, or defaults to the common meaning, or forces clarification.

In dialectical debates, ambiguity can derail an argument unless handled systematically. This passage from Adab al-Bahth teaches how to treat ambiguous words: either a contextual clue (qarina) selects the intended meaning, or the more common meaning is presumed. If neither works, the speaker must clarify. This lesson equips you to spot these situations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous term
When the questioner objects, locate the word that has multiple possible meanings. For example, 'ayn could mean 'eye' or 'spring'. That word is the source of ambiguity.
2
Check for a contextual clue (qarina)
Examine the surrounding speech for a word that narrows down the meaning. In the example, 'shurb' (drinking) points to 'spring' because drinking involves water, not eyes.
The qarina must be explicit and logically connected.
3
Apply the rule of preponderance if no clue
If no qarina exists, take the word in its more common meaning when stripped of context. For example, 'asad' is more often used for the animal than for a brave man.
This rule applies when one meaning is clearly more frequent.
4
Admit or demand clarification
If the speaker admits his expression is unfamiliar (gharaba) or ambiguous (ijmal), he must clarify. Otherwise, the previous steps determine the meaning.
Clarification is mandatory; otherwise the debate cannot proceed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تمنع الشرب من عينك. فيقول :المستفسر ما مرادك بالعين؟ فيقول : ليس في كلامي إجمال؛ لأنه لا يحتمل إلا معنى واحدا. فيقول المستفسر : أليس لفظ العين في كلامك مشتركا بين الباصرة والجارية وغيرهما؟ فيقول المعلل : لفظ الشرب في كلامي قرينة تعيّن أن مرادي العين الجارية لا الباصرة ولا غيرها فلا يحتمل كلامي إلا معنى واحدا هو العين الجارية . الثاني : ترجيح الاحتمال الذي قصده على غيره من الاحتمالات كأن يقول المعلل : ذلك الشبح أسد فيقول المستفسر : ما مرادك بالأسد ؟ فيقول : لا إجمال في كلامي فيقول الآخر: أليس الأسد يطلق على الحيوان المفترس وعلى الرجل الشجاع؟ فيقول الآخر : هو في الحيوان المفترس أظهرُ منه في الرجل الشجاع عند التجرد من القرائن، وحمل اللفظ على الأظهر لازم؛ لرجحانه على غيره . وإذا كان المعلل معترفًا بالغرابة أو الإجمال لزمه بيان مراده من كلامه .
You forbid drinking from your 'ayn. The questioner says: What do you mean by 'ayn? He says: There is no ambiguity in my speech because it only has one meaning. The questioner says: Is not the word 'ayn in your speech shared between the eye, the spring, and others? The reasoner says: The word 'shurb' (drinking) in my speech is a clue that specifies that I mean the spring, not the eye or others, so my speech only has one meaning, which is the spring. Second: giving preponderance to the intended probability over other probabilities, as when the reasoner says: That apparition is an 'asad. The questioner says: What do you mean by 'asad? He says: There is no ambiguity in my speech. The other says: Is not 'asad applied to the predatory animal and to the brave man? The other says: It is more apparent in the predatory animal than in the brave man when stripped of clues, and applying the word to the more apparent is necessary due to its preponderance over other meanings. And if the reasoner admits unfamiliarity or ambiguity, he must clarify his intended meaning from his speech.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is a procedural guide for handling ambiguous or unfamiliar expressions in dialectical debate. It begins with an example where the reasoner uses the word 'ayn, which can mean 'eye' or 'spring'. The questioner asks for clarification, and the reasoner explains that the context (qarina) of 'drinking' determines the meaning as 'spring'. The author then presents a second method: when a word has multiple meanings but one is more common, the word is taken in its more apparent meaning unless there is a clue to the contrary. The example is 'asad, which can mean 'lion' or 'brave man'; in the absence of clues, the former is preponderant. Finally, if the reasoner admits that his expression is unfamiliar (gharaba) or ambiguous (ijmal), he is obliged to clarify his intended meaning.

Key Terms
إجمال (ijmal): The quality of being ambiguous, i.e., having more than one possible meaning.
غرابة (gharaba): Unfamiliarity or obscurity of an expression, such that its meaning is not readily understood.
قرينة (qarina): A contextual clue that specifies the intended meaning among multiple possibilities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
When ambiguity arises, first seek a contextual clue; if none, apply the preponderant meaning; otherwise, clarification is mandatory.

Classifying Claims and the Initial Response
Author outlines the questioner's duties: checking source, nature of claim, and initial response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a disputant's claim as self-evident or theoretical and decide whether to accept it, deny it, or demand evidence.

Core Idea
A claim's epistemic status determines the questioner's first move.

In dialectical debate, the questioner must not object randomly. After the disputant's statement is clarified, the questioner must first investigate the source and nature of the claim. This lesson walks you through that initial screening process, from checking transmission to classifying the claim as badihi or nazari.

Guided Path
1
Determine the claim's origin
Check if the disputant is transmitting the claim from a source or producing it himself. If transmitting without commitment to correctness, the only valid move is to demand verification of the transmission.
2
Classify the claim as badihi or nazari
If the disputant is the originator or a committed transmitter, identify whether the claim is self-evident (badihi) or theoretical (nazari). For badihi, further distinguish between clear (jali) and hidden (khafi).
3
Respond based on classification and evidence
For clear self-evident claims, accept them or be considered obstinate. For theoretical or hidden self-evident claims, check if evidence (dalil) or alert (tanbih) has been provided. If not, deny the claim and request the evidence. If evidence is present, move to the objection phase.
This step sets the stage for the three objections covered in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كان لفظ صاحب التصديق ظاهر المعنى واضح الكلمات لا إجمال فيه ولا غرابة أو كان فيه إجمال أو غرابة وبينه صاحبه حتى صار واضح المعنى لا إشكال فيه من غرابة أو إجمال = فعليه بعد هذه الخطوة أن ينظر في كلام المعلل صاحب التصديق : هل هو ناقل لذلك التصديق عن غيره من كتاب أو راو؟ أو هو آت به من تلقاء نفسه؟ وفي حال كونه ناقلاً له عن غيره : هل هو ملتزم صحته أو ليس ملتزما لها؟ فإن كان ناقلاً ولم يلتزم صحة ما نقل فليس له أن يوجه إليه شيئًا إلا المطالبة بتصحيح النقل، ويلزمه حينئذ تصحيح نقله بإثباته ذلك بطريق من طرق الإثبات . وأما إن كان جاء بالتصديق من عند نفسه أو نقله والتزم صحته فعلى السائل أن يفعل كما يأتي : وهو أن ينظر في ذلك التصديق هل هو بديهي أو نظري ؟ وإذا كان بديهيا فهل هو جلي أو خفي؟ فإن وجد التصديق بديهيا جليًا فعليه تسليمه والإذعانُ له، وإن منعه فهو مكابر . وإن وجد التصديق نظريًا أو بديهيا خفيًا فلينظر : هل أقام صاحب التصديق البديهي الخفي عليه تنبيها أو لم يقمه عليه ؟ وهل أقام صاحب التصديق النظري عليه دليلاً أو لم يقمه؟ فإن كان صاحب النظري لم يقم عليه دليلاً، وصاحب البديهي الخفي لم يقم عليه تنبيها ، بل كلاهما دعوى مجردة فليس للسائل إلا شيء واحد وهو منع الدعوى المذكورة وطلب الدليل عليها أو التنبيه، وعلى صاحب الدعوى أن يأتي بالدليل في النظري، أو التنبيه في البديهي الخفي . فإن كان صاحب التصديق الذي هو الدعوى قد أقام عليه دليلاً إن كان نظريًا أو تنبيها إن كان بديهيا خفيًا، فللسائل حينئذ أن يعترض بإحدى الوظائف الثلاث الموضحة سابقا، وهي (المنع) وهو المناقضة والنقض التفصيلي، و (النقض) ويقال له النقض الإجمالي و(المعارضة)، وقد تقدم إيضاح الجميع وإقسامة وأمثلته، وعلى صاحب الدعوى بعد ورود أحد هذه الاعتراضات أن يجيب عنه، وقد بينا أوجه جوابه عن كل واحد منها والعلم عند الله - تعالى -.
And if the wording of the possessor of the assertion is clear in meaning, plain in words, having no ambiguity or unfamiliarity, or if it had ambiguity or unfamiliarity but the possessor clarified it until it became clear in meaning with no problem of unfamiliarity or ambiguity, then after this step he must look at the speech of the reasoner, the possessor of the assertion: Is he transmitting that assertion from someone else, from a book or a narrator? Or is he producing it from himself? And in the case of him transmitting it from someone else: Does he commit to its correctness or not? If he is transmitting and does not commit to the correctness of what he transmitted, then he may not direct anything towards him except to demand correction of the transmission, and he must then correct his transmission by proving it through one of the methods of proof. But if he produced the assertion from himself or transmitted it and committed to its correctness, then the questioner must do as follows: He must look at that assertion: is it self-evident (badihi) or theoretical (nazari)? And if it is self-evident, is it clear (jali) or hidden (khafi)? If he finds the assertion to be clearly self-evident, he must submit to it and assent to it, and if he denies it, he is obstinate. If he finds the assertion to be theoretical or a hidden self-evident, then he must look: Did the possessor of the assertion—if hidden self-evident—present an alert (tanbih) upon it or not? And did the possessor of the theoretical assertion present a proof (dalil) upon it or not? If the possessor of the theoretical did not present a proof, and the possessor of the hidden self-evident did not present an alert, but both are a mere claim, then the questioner has only one thing: to deny the mentioned claim and request the proof or the alert. And the possessor of the claim must bring the proof for the theoretical, or the alert for the hidden self-evident. If the possessor of the assertion—the claim—has presented a proof if it is theoretical, or an alert if it is hidden self-evident, then the questioner may object with one of the three functions previously explained, which are: al-man' (denial), which is the detailed contradiction and refutation; al-naqd (refutation), which is called the general refutation; and al-mu'arada (counter-argument). The explanation of all of them, their divisions, and their examples have preceded. And the possessor of the claim, after one of these objections occurs, must respond to it. And we have explained the aspects of his answer concerning each one of them. And knowledge is with Allah, the Exalted.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is a detailed procedural guide for the questioner in dialectical debate after the disputant's claim has been clarified. It begins by stating that if the claim is now clear, the questioner must first determine whether the disputant is transmitting the claim from another source or producing it himself. If transmitting without committing to its correctness, the only valid response is to demand correction of the transmission through proof. If the disputant is the originator or committed transmitter, the questioner then classifies the claim as self-evident (badihi) or theoretical (nazari). For clearly self-evident claims, the questioner must accept them or be considered obstinate. For theoretical or hidden self-evident claims, the questioner checks if evidence (dalil) or alert (tanbih) has been provided. If not, the questioner may simply deny the claim and demand evidence. If evidence is provided, the questioner can raise one of three objections: man' (denial), naqd (refutation), or mu'arada (counter-argument). The author notes that these have been explained earlier, and the disputant must then answer. This passage thus establishes a structured method for advancing dialectical inquiry, ensuring that objections are only raised after properly evaluating the nature and support of the claim.

Key Terms
بديهي (badihi): A proposition that is self-evident, i.e., accepted without need for proof.
نظري (nazari): A proposition that requires proof or reasoning to be established.
منع (man'): An objection by denying the claim or its premise, often detailed contradiction.
نقض (naqd): An objection by refuting the claim through a counterexample or inconsistency, often general.
معارضة (mu'arada): An objection by presenting a counter-argument that opposes the claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Immediately raise a mu'arada
B) Deny the claim and request evidence
C) Accept the claim as self-evident
D) Demand the source of transmission

Key Takeaway
The questioner must first establish the epistemic status and evidentiary support of a claim before any objection.
