The Three Objections: Man', Naqd, and Mu'arada
Author outlines the questioner's duties: checking source, nature of claim, and types of objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between man', naqd, and mu'arada and apply them appropriately in a dialectical debate.

Core Idea
Man', naqd, and mu'arada are the three licensed objections after evidence is presented.

Once the disputant provides evidence for a theoretical or hidden self-evident claim, the questioner may raise a structured objection. This lesson unpacks the three types: man' (detailed denial), naqd (general refutation), and mu'arada (counter-argument), as used in the Islamic dialectical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the three objection types
The passage lists man', naqd, and mu'arada. Man' is a detailed contradiction that denies a premise or the conclusion. Naqd is a general refutation that undermines the proof as a whole. Mu'arada is presenting a counter-argument that conflicts with the claim.
2
Differentiate man' from naqd
Man' targets a specific part of the argument, while naqd attacks the overall validity. Man' is often 'detailed contradiction' (naqd tafṣīlī) and naqd is 'general refutation' (naqd ijmalī).
3
Understand the role of mu'arada
Mu'arada does not directly refute but offers an alternative argument that, if accepted, would make the original claim problematic. The disputant must then respond.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كان لفظ صاحب التصديق ظاهر المعنى واضح الكلمات لا إجمال فيه ولا غرابة أو كان فيه إجمال أو غرابة وبينه صاحبه حتى صار واضح المعنى لا إشكال فيه من غرابة أو إجمال = فعليه بعد هذه الخطوة أن ينظر في كلام المعلل صاحب التصديق : هل هو ناقل لذلك التصديق عن غيره من كتاب أو راو؟ أو هو آت به من تلقاء نفسه؟ وفي حال كونه ناقلاً له عن غيره : هل هو ملتزم صحته أو ليس ملتزما لها؟ فإن كان ناقلاً ولم يلتزم صحة ما نقل فليس له أن يوجه إليه شيئًا إلا المطالبة بتصحيح النقل، ويلزمه حينئذ تصحيح نقله بإثباته ذلك بطريق من طرق الإثبات . وأما إن كان جاء بالتصديق من عند نفسه أو نقله والتزم صحته فعلى السائل أن يفعل كما يأتي : وهو أن ينظر في ذلك التصديق هل هو بديهي أو نظري ؟ وإذا كان بديهيا فهل هو جلي أو خفي؟ فإن وجد التصديق بديهيا جليًا فعليه تسليمه والإذعانُ له، وإن منعه فهو مكابر . وإن وجد التصديق نظريًا أو بديهيا خفيًا فلينظر : هل أقام صاحب التصديق البديهي الخفي عليه تنبيها أو لم يقمه عليه ؟ وهل أقام صاحب التصديق النظري عليه دليلاً أو لم يقمه؟ فإن كان صاحب النظري لم يقم عليه دليلاً، وصاحب البديهي الخفي لم يقم عليه تنبيها ، بل كلاهما دعوى مجردة فليس للسائل إلا شيء واحد وهو منع الدعوى المذكورة وطلب الدليل عليها أو التنبيه، وعلى صاحب الدعوى أن يأتي بالدليل في النظري، أو التنبيه في البديهي الخفي . فإن كان صاحب التصديق الذي هو الدعوى قد أقام عليه دليلاً إن كان نظريًا أو تنبيها إن كان بديهيا خفيًا، فللسائل حينئذ أن يعترض بإحدى الوظائف الثلاث الموضحة سابقا، وهي (المنع) وهو المناقضة والنقض التفصيلي، و (النقض) ويقال له النقض الإجمالي و(المعارضة)، وقد تقدم إيضاح الجميع وإقسامة وأمثلته، وعلى صاحب الدعوى بعد ورود أحد هذه الاعتراضات أن يجيب عنه، وقد بينا أوجه جوابه عن كل واحد منها والعلم عند الله - تعالى -.
And if the wording of the possessor of the assertion is clear in meaning, plain in words, having no ambiguity or unfamiliarity, or if it had ambiguity or unfamiliarity but the possessor clarified it until it became clear in meaning with no problem of unfamiliarity or ambiguity, then after this step he must look at the speech of the reasoner, the possessor of the assertion: Is he transmitting that assertion from someone else, from a book or a narrator? Or is he producing it from himself? And in the case of him transmitting it from someone else: Does he commit to its correctness or not? If he is transmitting and does not commit to the correctness of what he transmitted, then he may not direct anything towards him except to demand correction of the transmission, and he must then correct his transmission by proving it through one of the methods of proof. But if he produced the assertion from himself or transmitted it and committed to its correctness, then the questioner must do as follows: He must look at that assertion: is it self-evident (badihi) or theoretical (nazari)? And if it is self-evident, is it clear (jali) or hidden (khafi)? If he finds the assertion to be clearly self-evident, he must submit to it and assent to it, and if he denies it, he is obstinate. If he finds the assertion to be theoretical or a hidden self-evident, then he must look: Did the possessor of the assertion—if hidden self-evident—present an alert (tanbih) upon it or not? And did the possessor of the theoretical assertion present a proof (dalil) upon it or not? If the possessor of the theoretical did not present a proof, and the possessor of the hidden self-evident did not present an alert, but both are a mere claim, then the questioner has only one thing: to deny the mentioned claim and request the proof or the alert. And the possessor of the claim must bring the proof for the theoretical, or the alert for the hidden self-evident. If the possessor of the assertion—the claim—has presented a proof if it is theoretical, or an alert if it is hidden self-evident, then the questioner may object with one of the three functions previously explained, which are: al-man' (denial), which is the detailed contradiction and refutation; al-naqd (refutation), which is called the general refutation; and al-mu'arada (counter-argument). The explanation of all of them, their divisions, and their examples have preceded. And the possessor of the claim, after one of these objections occurs, must respond to it. And we have explained the aspects of his answer concerning each one of them. And knowledge is with Allah, the Exalted.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is a detailed procedural guide for the questioner in dialectical debate after the disputant's claim has been clarified. It begins by stating that if the claim is now clear, the questioner must first determine whether the disputant is transmitting the claim from another source or producing it himself. If transmitting without committing to its correctness, the only valid response is to demand correction of the transmission through proof. If the disputant is the originator or committed transmitter, the questioner then classifies the claim as self-evident (badihi) or theoretical (nazari). For clearly self-evident claims, the questioner must accept them or be considered obstinate. For theoretical or hidden self-evident claims, the questioner checks if evidence (dalil) or alert (tanbih) has been provided. If not, the questioner may simply deny the claim and demand evidence. If evidence is provided, the questioner can raise one of three objections: man' (denial), naqd (refutation), or mu'arada (counter-argument). The author notes that these have been explained earlier, and the disputant must then answer. This passage thus establishes a structured method for advancing dialectical inquiry, ensuring that objections are only raised after properly evaluating the nature and support of the claim.

Key Terms
بديهي (badihi): A proposition that is self-evident, i.e., accepted without need for proof.
نظري (nazari): A proposition that requires proof or reasoning to be established.
منع (man'): An objection by denying the claim or its premise, often detailed contradiction.
نقض (naqd): An objection by refuting the claim through a counterexample or inconsistency, often general.
معارضة (mu'arada): An objection by presenting a counter-argument that opposes the claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which objection is described as a detailed contradiction that denies a specific premise?
A) Man'
B) Naqd
C) Mu'arada
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The three objections provide a structured way to challenge arguments after evidence is given, ensuring disciplined debate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which objection type (man', naqd, or mu'arada) is being used in this exchange: Disputant claims 'Prayer is obligatory because of the Quranic verse.' Questioner says 'Your evidence is insufficient because the verse is ambiguous.'
?
Construct a mu'arada objection against the claim that 'All intoxicants are forbidden' using the example of nabidh (date drink).

Naql: Definition and Types of Transmission in Debate
Defines naql and its two types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a committed and uncommitted transmission (naql) and identify the implications for debate.

Core Idea
Naql becomes a claim only when the transmitter asserts its truth.

In debate, citing authorities is common, but not all citations carry the same weight. This lesson unpacks the concept of naql (transmission) and its two key types: when the transmitter commits to the truth of the transmitted statement, making it a claim, and when he does not, leaving it as a mere citation. Understanding this distinction is crucial for knowing how to respond to an opponent's citation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition of naql
In the Arabic, find the definition: 'النقل هو أن تذكر كلاما لغيرك مع بيانك إسناده لمن نقلته عنه'. This is the operational definition: mentioning someone else's statement with attribution.
This definition focuses on the act of quoting, not on verifying truth.
2
Spot the two scenarios based on commitment
After the definition, the author splits naql into two cases: (1) the transmitter commits to the truth of what he transmitted (يلتزم صحة ما نقل), and (2) he does not commit (لم يلتزم صحة ما نقل). Find these phrases in the Arabic.
Commitment turns the transmission into a claim (da'wa).
3
Recognize the consequence of commitment
If the transmitter commits, he becomes a mudda'i (claimant), and the transmission is treated as a claim. Then all rules for assents (tasdiq) apply, meaning the opponent can object in standard ways.
This shifts the burden of proof onto the transmitter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في النقل
اعلم أن النقل هو أن تذكر كلاما لغيرك مع بيانك إسناده لمن نقلته عنه، كقولك : قال مالك والشافعي إن النية شرط في صحة الوضوء، وقال أبو حنيفة بعكس ذلك .
وسواء كان ذلك الكلام المنقول تصديقا كما مثلنا، أو تعريفا أو تقسيما أو غير ذلك، فإن التزم الناقل صحة ما نقل كأن يقول : (وما قاله مالك والشافعي هو الصحيح في المثال المذكور، أو عكس ذلك، فهو مُدّع، وهذا الذي قاله دعوى فيجري فيه جميع ما ذكرناه في التصديق .
وإن كان لم يلتزم صحة ما نقل : فإن كان بديهيا أو مسلّما عند الخصم أو معتبرًا من ضروريات مذهبه لم يتوجه إليه شيء من الاعتراضات، ولزم خصمه القبول والتسليم، وإن كان ليس كذلك فلا يتوجه إليه إلا المطالبة بتصحيح النقل بطريق يثبت بها صحة ذلك النقل، وقد يسمى طلب تصحيح النقل منع الدعوى .
وقد قدمنا في أول الكلام في البحث والمناظرة أن الصواب مطالبته تصحيح النقل، خلافًا لمن زعم أن النقل ليس محلاً للمناظرة أصلاً .
واختلف أهل هذا الفن في تصحيح النقل على القول به : هل هو واجب أو مستحسن؟ فقيل يجب، وقيل يستحسن، وقيل: إن كان السائل جاهلاً بصحة النقل وجب طلب تصحيحه، وإن كان عالما بأن النقل صحيح وأن الناقل صادق في نقله لم يجز له طلب التصحيح، وهذا ظاهر .
Section on Transmission (Naql)
Know that naql is that you mention a statement from someone else while clarifying its attribution to the one from whom you transmitted it, like your saying: "Malik and al-Shafi'i said that intention is a condition for the validity of wudu', and Abu Hanifa said the opposite."
And whether that transmitted statement is an assent (tasdiq) as we exemplified, or a definition (ta'rif) or a division (taqsim) or something else: if the transmitter commits to the truth of what he transmitted, as if he says: "What Malik and al-Shafi'i said is correct in the mentioned example, or the opposite," then he is a claimant (mudda'i), and what he said is a claim (da'wa), and all that we mentioned concerning assent applies to it.
But if he does not commit to the truth of what he transmitted: then if it is self-evident (badihi) or conceded (musallam) by the opponent or considered among the necessities of his school, then none of the objections (i'tiradat) apply to it, and his opponent must accept and submit. And if it is not like that, then nothing applies to it except the demand (mutalaba) to verify the transmission in a way that establishes the truth of that transmission. And the demand to verify the transmission may be called "rejection of the claim" (man' al-da'wa).
And we have already stated at the beginning of our discussion on research and debate that the correct view is to demand from him the verification of the transmission, contrary to those who claim that transmission is not a locus for debate at all.
And the practitioners of this art disagreed regarding the verification of transmission, upon the acceptance of it: is it obligatory (wajib) or recommended (mustahsan)? Some said it is obligatory, some said it is recommended, and others said: if the questioner is ignorant of the truth of the transmission, then it is obligatory to demand its verification; but if he knows that the transmission is true and that the transmitter is truthful in his transmission, then it is not permissible for him to demand verification. And this is evident.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is from a work on the art of debate (adab al-bahth). It defines a key concept: naql (transmission) — when a debater quotes another authority. The author begins by defining naql as mentioning someone else's statement while clarifying its attribution. The example given is from fiqh: quoting Imam Malik and al-Shafi'i on the requirement of intention in wudu', and contrasting with Abu Hanifa.

The author then distinguishes two scenarios. First, if the transmitter explicitly asserts the truth of what he transmitted (e.g., saying that Malik and al-Shafi'i are correct), then he becomes a mudda'i (claimant) and his statement is a da'wa (claim). In that case, all the rules that apply to assents (tasdiq) apply here, meaning the opponent can object in the same ways as to any claim.

Second, if the transmitter does not commit to the truth of the transmitted statement, then its status depends on the nature of the statement. If the transmitted statement is self-evident (badihi), or conceded by the opponent (musallam), or a necessary part of the opponent's own madhhab (e.g., a foundational principle), then no objections can be directed against the transmission itself; the opponent must accept it. Otherwise, the only permissible objection is to demand verification (tas-hih al-naql) — i.e., to ask the transmitter to prove that his transmission is accurate. This demand is sometimes called "man' al-da'wa" (rejection of the claim), though here it's not a claim but a request to verify the source.

The author then references an earlier discussion where he stated the correct position: the opponent is entitled to demand verification of the transmission, contrary to some who thought that transmission is not a proper subject of debate at all.

Finally, the author notes a disagreement among scholars of this field regarding whether verifying a transmission is obligatory (wajib) or merely recommended (mustahsan). He presents three views: one says it is wajib, one says mustahsan, and a third distinguishes based on the knowledge of the questioner: if the questioner does not know whether the transmission is accurate, he must demand verification; but if he already knows it is true and the narrator is truthful, he may not demand it. The author finds this third view evident.

Key Terms
النقل (al-naql): Mentioning someone else's statement with attribution; a key operation in debate where one cites an authority. [Note: In hadith studies, naql has a more technical meaning of transmission of hadith; here it is broader.]
مُدّع (mudda'i): A transmitter who asserts the truth of the transmitted statement, thus turning the naql into a claim. [Note: Stable in jadal literature.]
دعوى (da'wa): A statement that asserts a truth claim, subject to objection and proof. [Note: Stable in jadal literature.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if a transmitter commits to the truth of the transmitted statement?
A) The transmission is considered self-evident.
B) The transmitter becomes a claimant and the statement is treated as a claim.
C) The opponent must accept the transmission without objection.
D) The transmission is no longer subject to debate.

Key Takeaway
Naql is a citation; it becomes a claim only when the transmitter asserts its truth, shifting the burden of proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Given the following statement from a debate: 'Al-Shafi'i said that the basmalah is part of Surah al-Fatiha.' The transmitter adds: 'And this is the soundest opinion.' Is this a committed or uncommitted transmission? What consequence does this have for the opponent?
?
Task 2: In a debate on the attributes of Allah, a debater transmits: 'Ibn Taymiyya said that istiwa' is literal.' The transmitter does not indicate agreement. How should the opponent treat this transmission based on the rules of naql?

Objecting to Uncommitted Naql: The Demand for Verification
Rules for objecting to uncommitted naql

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when an opponent may demand verification of an uncommitted transmission and understand the different scholarly views on the obligation of such verification.

Core Idea
Only uncommitted transmissions that are not self-evident or conceded can be challenged by demanding verification.

When a transmitter does not commit to the truth of a citation, the opponent's options are limited. This lesson examines the conditions under which an uncommitted transmission is immune to objection (when it is self-evident, conceded, or a necessity of the opponent's school) and when the only permissible objection is to demand verification of the transmission. The lesson also covers the scholarly disagreement on whether such verification is obligatory or merely recommended.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of immune uncommitted transmissions
In the Arabic, find the three cases where an uncommitted transmission cannot be objected to: when it is 'بديهيا' (self-evident), 'مسلّما عند الخصم' (conceded by the opponent), or 'معتبرًا من ضروريات مذهبه' (a necessary part of the opponent's school).
In these cases, the opponent must accept and submit (لزم خصمه القبول والتسليم).
2
Recognize the only permissible objection for non-immune transmissions
If the transmission is not in those three categories, the opponent can only demand verification of the transmission ('المطالبة بتصحيح النقل'). This is the sole objection allowed.
The passage notes that this demand is sometimes called 'منع الدعوى' (rejection of the claim).
3
Analyze the three scholarly positions on the obligation of verification
The passage ends by presenting three views: verification is wajib, mustahsan, or conditional (depending on the questioner's knowledge).
The author considers the conditional view as evident ('ظاهر').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في النقل
اعلم أن النقل هو أن تذكر كلاما لغيرك مع بيانك إسناده لمن نقلته عنه، كقولك : قال مالك والشافعي إن النية شرط في صحة الوضوء، وقال أبو حنيفة بعكس ذلك .
وسواء كان ذلك الكلام المنقول تصديقا كما مثلنا، أو تعريفا أو تقسيما أو غير ذلك، فإن التزم الناقل صحة ما نقل كأن يقول : (وما قاله مالك والشافعي هو الصحيح في المثال المذكور، أو عكس ذلك، فهو مُدّع، وهذا الذي قاله دعوى فيجري فيه جميع ما ذكرناه في التصديق .
وإن كان لم يلتزم صحة ما نقل : فإن كان بديهيا أو مسلّما عند الخصم أو معتبرًا من ضروريات مذهبه لم يتوجه إليه شيء من الاعتراضات، ولزم خصمه القبول والتسليم، وإن كان ليس كذلك فلا يتوجه إليه إلا المطالبة بتصحيح النقل بطريق يثبت بها صحة ذلك النقل، وقد يسمى طلب تصحيح النقل منع الدعوى .

Section on Transmission (Naql)
Know that naql is that you mention a statement from someone else while clarifying its attribution to the one from whom you transmitted it, like your saying: "Malik and al-Shafi'i said that intention is a condition for the validity of wudu', and Abu Hanifa said the opposite."
And whether that transmitted statement is an assent (tasdiq) as we exemplified, or a definition (ta'rif) or a division (taqsim) or something else: if the transmitter commits to the truth of what he transmitted, as if he says: "What Malik and al-Shafi'i said is correct in the mentioned example, or the opposite," then he is a claimant (mudda'i), and what he said is a claim (da'wa), and all that we mentioned concerning assent applies to it.
But if he does not commit to the truth of what he transmitted: then if it is self-evident (badihi) or conceded (musallam) by the opponent or considered among the necessities of his school, then none of the objections (i'tiradat) apply to it, and his opponent must accept and submit. And if it is not like that, then nothing applies to it except the demand (mutalaba) to verify the transmission in a way that establishes the truth of that transmission. And the demand to verify the transmission may be called "rejection of the claim" (man' al-da'wa).
And we have already stated at the beginning of our discussion on research and debate that the correct view is to demand from him the verification of the transmission, contrary to those who claim that transmission is not a locus for debate at all.
And the practitioners of this art disagreed regarding the verification of transmission, upon the acceptance of it: is it obligatory (wajib) or recommended (mustahsan)? Some said it is obligatory, some said it is recommended, and others said: if the questioner is ignorant of the truth of the transmission, then it is obligatory to demand its verification; but if he knows that the transmission is true and that the transmitter is truthful in his transmission, then it is not permissible for him to demand verification. And this is evident.

Commentary

The author finds this third view evident.

Key Terms
المطالبة بتصحيح النقل (al-mutalaba bi-tas-hih al-naql): The demand to verify the accuracy of a transmission; an objection type specific to naql when the transmitter does not commit to its truth.
منع الدعوى (man' al-da'wa): An alternate term for demanding verification of transmission, used when the transmitter's statement is not a claim but a transmission.
بديهي (badihi): A proposition that is self-evident and requires no proof.
مسلّم (musallam): A proposition that is conceded or accepted by the opponent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the only permissible objection to an uncommitted transmission that is not self-evident, conceded, or a necessity of the opponent's school?
A) The opponent may reject the transmission outright.
B) The opponent may demand verification of the transmission.
C) The opponent may claim the transmitter is lying.
D) The opponent must accept the transmission.

Key Takeaway
The only objection to a non-immune uncommitted transmission is to demand its verification, and scholars differ on whether this verification is obligatory or recommended.

Objection on Pronoun Antecedent Order: Rank Precedence
Objection and answer about a feminine pronoun appearing before its antecedent, citing Ibn Malik's rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and evaluate a jadal exchange about linguistic precedence, applying the concept of 'rutba' (rank) to defend a grammatical construction.

Core Idea
A pronoun's antecedent can be delayed in wording if it is advanced in syntactic rank.

In this lesson, you will follow a typical dialectical exchange from the adab al-bahth tradition. The objector challenges a line of poetry by Dhu ar-Rumma based on a surface-level grammatical rule. The answerer invokes a deeper principle: precedence in rank (rutba) suffices to resolve the apparent violation. By the end, you will be able to apply this principle to similar linguistic and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
Read the objector's statement carefully. He argues that Dhu ar-Rumma's line is erroneous because it violates the rule that the pronoun's explicans must be mentioned before the pronoun. In the line, the feminine pronoun in 'dhayliha' appears before its apparent antecedent 'aṣ-ṣabā'.
The objector is relying on a surface-level word order rule.
2
Extract the answerer's defense
The answerer counters by introducing the concept of rutba (rank). He explains that even though 'aṣ-ṣabā' is delayed in wording, it is the subject (fa'il) and thus has a higher rank, making it logically prior. The pronoun effectively refers to something that is already 'present' in the syntactic structure.
This defense shifts the argument from word order to syntactic hierarchy.
3
Analyze the cited authority
The answerer cites Ibn Malik's line from al-Khulasa: 'Wa shā'a nahwa khāfa rabbahu 'Umar' (And common is like 'khāfa rabbahu 'Umar'). This example shows a pronoun (hu in rabbahu) referring back to 'Umar, which appears later. The answerer uses this as a precedent that such constructions are acceptable in Arabic.
The citation strengthens the defense by showing it aligns with established grammatical practice.
4
Assess the exchange by jadal rules
Evaluate whether the answerer's response successfully refutes the objection. The objector's rule was too literal; the answerer provides a principled exception. According to jadal rules, the answerer has effectively neutralized the challenge by offering a valid counter-argument supported by authority.
In jadal, an objection is refuted if the respondent can show the objection does not apply due to a relevant distinction—here, the concept of rank.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كأن يقول السائل في قول ذي الرمة : أمن دمنة جرّت بها ذيلها الصَّبا لصيداء - مهلاً - ماء عينيك سافح إنه خطأ؛ لمخالفته القاعدة العربية : وهي أن مفسّر الضمير يكون مذكورًا قبل الضمير، وهنا جاء بالضمير الذي هو ضمير الغائبة الواحدة في لفظة (ذيلها) قبل مفسِّره ، وهو لفظة (الصَّبا ) ؛ لأن الأصل : جرَّت الصَّبا ذيلها بها . فيقول المجيب : هذا ليس مخالفًا للقاعدة العربية ؛ لأن الفاعل وإن تأخر لفظًا فهو متقدم رتبة، فكأنه مذكور قبل الضمير ؛ لتقدمه في الرتبة، كما قال ابن مالك في الخلاصة : وشاع نحو خاف ربه عمر وشذ نحو زان نوره الشجر
Like when the questioner says regarding the saying of Dhu ar-Rumma: 'Is it from a relic that the east wind dragged its tail for Sayda? Gently! Your eye's water is flowing' it is an error; because it violates the Arabic rule: that the explicans of the pronoun must be mentioned before the pronoun, and here he came with the pronoun which is the feminine singular third person pronoun in the word (dhayliha) before its explicans, which is the word (as-ṣabā); because the original is: 'Jarrat aṣ-ṣabā dhaylahā bihā.' So the answerer says: This is not violating the Arabic rule; because the subject (fāʿil) even if it is delayed in wording, it is advanced in rank, so it is as if it is mentioned before the pronoun due to its precedence in rank, as Ibn Malik said in al-Khulāṣa: 'And common is like 'khāfa rabbahu ʿUmar' and rare is like 'zāna nūruhu ash-shajar'.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage presents a typical dialectical exchange (jadal) regarding a line of poetry by Dhu ar-Rumma. The objector (sā’il) claims that the line contains a grammatical error: the feminine pronoun in 'dhayliha' (its tail) appears before its explicit antecedent 'aṣ-ṣabā' (the east wind). According to the rule, the explicans (mufassir) of a pronoun must be mentioned prior to the pronoun itself. The original implied order is 'Jarrat aṣ-ṣabā dhaylahā bihā', where the subject 'aṣ-ṣabā' comes first. The answerer (mujīb) counters by invoking the principle of precedence in rank (al-taʿaddī fī al-rutba). Although the word 'aṣ-ṣabā' comes later in the actual wording (lafẓ), it is the subject (fāʿil) and thus has a higher rank (rutba) that makes it logically prior. This is analogous to the common Arabic construction 'khāfa rabbahu ʿUmar' (ʿUmar feared his Lord), where the pronoun 'hu' in 'rabbahu' refers to 'ʿUmar' which is mentioned later, but grammatical tradition accepts it because the subject is advanced in rank. The answerer cites Ibn Mālik's Khalāṣa to support this. Thus, the objection is refuted.

Key Terms
الضمير
مفسّر الضمير
الرتبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the pronoun is not needed
B) Because the antecedent is mentioned later in wording but is advanced in rank as subject
C) Because the line is not poetry
D) Because Arabic allows pronoun before antecedent in all cases

Key Takeaway
The principle of rank (rutba) allows a pronoun's antecedent to be delayed in wording if it occupies a higher syntactic rank, a crucial tool in defending linguistic constructions in jadal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the Quranic verse: (وَلَقَدْ خَلَقْنَا الْإِنْسَانَ وَنَعْلَمُ مَا تُوَسْوِسُ بِهِ نَفْسُهُ) [Qaf 50:16]. Defending a Grammatical Usage: The Deleted Vocative Particle in al-Mutanabbi
Objection and answer about calling a demonstrative pronoun with a deleted calling particle, citing Kufan school.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how a debater defends a disputed linguistic usage by appealing to a recognized grammatical school in the adab al-bahth tradition.

Core Idea
In adab al-bahth, an objection is answered by citing an authoritative school that permits the disputed usage.

In dialectical exchanges about Arabic grammar, the objector may attack a poet's construction as violating the established rule. The answerer's task is not merely to claim correctness, but to ground the usage in a recognized scholarly madhhab. This lesson examines a concrete example from al-Mutanabbi where a demonstrative noun is used with a deleted vocative particle, and how the answerer defends it by appealing to the Kufan school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
Locate the specific line of al-Mutanabbi and the grammatical issue: 'hādhī barazt' (this appeared) is used as a vocative with a deleted calling particle (yā). The objector says this is an error because calling demonstrative nouns with a deleted particle violates the general rule.
2
Find the answerer's defence strategy
The answerer does not deny the deletion. Instead, he cites the Kufan school of grammar, which permits calling demonstrative nouns and generic nouns with a deleted particle. He also notes that al-Mutanabbi himself was a Kufan.
3
Analyse the citation from Ibn Malik
The answerer quotes a line from Ibn Malik's al-Khulāṣa: 'And that is rarely in the generic noun and the demonstrative; whoever prevents it, then support his opponent.' This shows that even among the Kufans, the usage is rare (qall), but it is not invalid.
4
Evaluate the strength of the defence
Consider whether citing a school's permission is sufficient. The answerer shifts the burden to the objector: if he denies the usage entirely, he must support his case. This is a common dialectical move—appealing to ikhtilāf (scholarly disagreement) to justify a marginal usage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكان يقول السائل في قول أبي الطيب المتنبي: هذي برزت لنا فهجتِ رسيسا ثم انثنيت وما شفيت نسيسا إنه خطأ؛ لأن فيه نداء اسم الإشارة في قوله (هذي برزتِ) بحرف محذوف ونداء أسماء الإشارة وأسماء الأجناس بأدوات محذوفة مخالف للقاعدة العربية . فيقول المجيب : نداء أسماء الإشارة وأسماء الأجناس بحرف محذوف هو مذهب الكوفيين والمتنبي كوفي كما قال ابن مالك في الخلاصة: وذاك في اسم الجنس والمشار له قل ومن يمنعه فانصر عادله
And the questioner would say regarding the saying of Abu at-Tayyib al-Mutanabbi: 'This (feminine) appeared to us and stirred an old love, then she turned away and did not cure my fever' it is an error; because it contains a vocative of a demonstrative noun in his saying 'hādhī barazt' with a deleted letter, and calling demonstrative nouns and generic nouns with deleted tools is contrary to the Arabic rule. So the answerer says: Calling demonstrative nouns and generic nouns with a deleted letter is the doctrine of the Kufans, and al-Mutanabbi is Kufan, as Ibn Malik said in al-Khulāṣa: 'And that is rarely in the generic noun and the demonstrative; whoever prevents it, then support his opponent.'
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
Here is another dialectical example. The objector attacks a line by al-Mutanabbi where he uses 'hādhī barazt' (this appeared) as a vocative with a hidden calling particle (e.g., yā). According to some grammarians, calling a demonstrative noun (ism ishāra) or a generic noun (ism jins) with a deleted particle is not allowed. The answerer defends it by citing the Kufan school, which permits such constructions. Al-Mutanabbi was a Kufan in grammar, so his usage is consistent. The answerer quotes Ibn Mālik's al-Khulāṣa where it says that these vocatives occur, though rarely (qall), and if someone denies it, he is wrong. This shows how the debater can justify an expression by appealing to a recognized grammatical madhhab.

نداء
اسم الإشارة
حرف محذوف

D) By citing the rule of precedence in rank

Key Takeaway
In adab al-bahth, a disputed linguistic usage can be defended by appealing to a recognized grammatical madhhab that permits it, thereby shifting the burden of proof to the objector.

Defending a Grammatical Usage with a Minority School
Objection and answer about raising the subject with lightened لَكِنَّ

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the objector's challenge and the answerer's defense in a dialectical exchange and evaluate the strength of citing a minority opinion.

Core Idea
Even a minority grammatical opinion can serve as a valid defense in scholarly debate.

In Islamic scholarly debates (munāẓara), it is not enough to simply state a position; one must also be prepared to defend it against objections. This passage presents a brief exchange concerning the lightened form of the particle lākinna. The objector claims that using it to raise the subject is a grammatical error, since the lightened form is normally inoperative. The answerer defends the usage by appealing to the authority of a respected grammarian who held a contrary view. This lesson will guide you through analyzing the structure of this dialectical move.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested claim
The claim under objection is that 'the subject is made accusative with lightened lākinna'. Locate this in the Arabic text.
The claim is: 'وكأن ينصب المبتدأ بلكن المخففة' (And like that he makes the subject accusative with lightened lākinna).
2
Identify the objection (iʿtirāḍ)
The objector argues that when lākinna is lightened, it becomes inoperative (uhmilat), so it cannot govern the accusative. Find the basis of this objection.
The objector says 'إن خُفّفت أهملت' (if it is lightened, it is inoperative) – this relies on the standard grammatical rule.
3
Identify the answer (jawāb)
The answerer does not deny the rule but instead cites an alternative school of thought. What is the answer?
The answerer says 'هو جار على مذهب يونس' (it is according to the school of Yunus) and cites al-Mukhtār ibn Būn's Red Alfiyya.
4
Assess the strength of the defense
Evaluate whether citing a minority opinion is a legitimate defense in dialectics. Consider the status of Yunus ibn Habib and the role of authoritative texts.
In jadal, citing a respected authority (even if a minority) can be acceptable, especially if the opponent recognizes that authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكأن ينصب المبتدأ بلكن المخففة، فيبطله السائل بأنها إن خُفّفت أهملت والنصبُ بها مخففة مخالف للقاعدة العربية . فيقول المجيب : هو جار على مذهب يونس كما قال الشيخ المختار بن بون في ألفيته الحمراء
And like that he makes the subject accusative with lightened lakinna; so the questioner invalidates it by saying that if it (lakinna) is lightened, it is inoperative, and making the subject accusative with lightened lakinna violates the Arabic rule. So the answerer says: That is according to the school of Yunus, as al-Mukhtār ibn Būn said in his Red Alfiyya.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
The final example addresses the use of the lightened form of lakinna (لاكن المخففة). Normally, lakinna is a particle that governs the accusative (like innna), but when lightened (by dropping the nūn), it becomes inoperative (al-qīlu muhmal) according to most grammarians, so it should not cause the subject to be in the accusative case. The objector argues that using it to raise the subject (actually, the text says 'yansibu al-mubtada' – makes the subject accusative) is an error. However, the answerer defends this usage by referencing the school of the Basran grammarian Yunus ibn Habib, who permitted it. The answerer cites al-Mukhtār ibn Būn's Red Alfiyya as support. This shows that even a minority opinion of a respected grammarian can serve as a valid defense in a munāzara.

Key Terms
لكن المخففة
مذهب يونس

Key Takeaway
In munāẓara, citing a respected grammarian's minority view can adequately defend a contested usage.

Analyzing a Dialectical Exchange on Vocative Grammar
Author rebuts objection to Al-Mutanabbi's vocative with demonstrative by citing Kufic school.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the parts of a dialectical exchange (mudda'i, mu'tarid, jawab) and evaluate how school allegiance is used to defend a grammatical claim.

Core Idea
Dialectic resolves grammar disputes by citing authoritative schools, not universal rules.

In adab al-bahth, objections and responses follow a structured pattern. Here, the questioner attacks al-Mutanabbi's line on grammatical grounds; the responder defends not by denying the rule but by showing the rule itself has exceptions according to a recognized school. This lesson unpacks that exchange so you can apply similar analysis to Islamic scholarly debates.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the original claim and the objection
Read the line by al-Mutanabbi. The questioner says it is an error because calling a demonstrative (هذي) with a deleted vocative particle violates the Arabic rule. Notice what exactly is being objected to: not just any usage, but a specific construction.
The objection targets the deletion of the vocative particle before the demonstrative.
2
2. Extract the defender's response (jawab)
The responder does not claim there is no rule; instead, he says that calling demonstratives and common nouns with a deleted particle is allowed by the Kufic school. He also cites Ibn Malik's verse to show that this usage is rare but permissible.
The defense relies on appealing to a recognized grammatical school.
3
3. Assess the dialectical strategy
The responder uses the authority of the Kufic school and the fact that al-Mutanabbi is a Kufan. This is a common tactic: if the claimant and the authority align, the objection loses force. Evaluate whether this strategy is valid within the rules of adab al-bahth.
Note that the questioner did not specify which school's rule he was invoking; the responder supplies the relevant school.
4
4. Relate to adab al-bahth rules
In proper dialectic, the questioner must state his objection clearly, and the responder must provide evidence. Here, the responder uses a secondary source (Ibn Malik's summary) as evidence. This shows how citations are used to support claims.
This exchange models the structured give-and-take of scholarly debate.

Source Text

The responder (المجيب) defends it by invoking the Kufic school of grammar, which permits calling upon demonstrative pronouns and common nouns with a deleted vocative particle. Al-Mutanabbi is a Kufan, so his usage is correct according to his school. The responder cites Ibn Malik's al-Khulasa to support this: 'وذاك في اسم الجنس والمشار له قل ومن يمنعه فانصر عادله' meaning that such calling is rare but permissible, and whoever prevents it should be opposed. Defending a Grammatical Exception by Authority in Jadal
Author rebuts objection to using lightened 'lākin' to raise subject by citing Yunus's school.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a dialectical objection in Arabic grammar by identifying the mu'tarid's claim, the jawab, and the authority cited.

Core Idea
In jadal, a universal rule can be challenged by citing a respected grammarian who permits the exception.

This passage presents a classic jadal exchange in Arabic grammar. The objector asserts that lightened 'lākin' cannot raise the subject because it is inoperative. The responder rebuts by citing the school of Yunus ibn Habib, a grammarian known for preserving anomalous usages. This shows how authority and reported usage can override a general grammatical rule in dialectical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Claim Under Attack
Find the grammatical rule that the objector treats as universal: lightened 'lākin' is inoperative and cannot govern case.
2
Locate the Objection
The objector argues that using lightened 'lākin' to raise the subject violates the Arabic rule because the lightened form is inoperative (uhmilat).
3
Find the Respondent's Defense Strategy
The responder does not deny the general rule but cites an exception: the school of Yunus allows it. He also quotes a poetic source (al-Mukhtar ibn Boun) and notes that al-Akhfash agreed.
4
Evaluate the Strength of the Authority
Yunus is a respected grammarian known for preserving rare usages. Citing him strengthens the jawab because it shows the usage is attested among the Arabs, not a mere innovation.
In jadal, the acceptability of an analogy depends on the strength of the source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكأن ينصب المبتدأ بلكن المخففة، فيبطله السائل بأنها إن خُفّفت أهملت والنصبُ بها مخففة مخالف للقاعدة العربية . فيقول المجيب : هو جار على مذهب يونس كما قال الشيخ المختار بن بون في ألفيته الحمراء: (لكن) إن خففتها فأهملا ويونس مجوز أن تعملا وعن يونس أنه حكى ذلك عن العرب، وبه قال الأخفش .
And as if raising the subject by the lightened 'lākin', the questioner invalidates it by saying that if it is lightened it becomes inoperative, and raising the subject with it while lightened violates the Arabic rule. So the responder says: This follows the school of Yunus, as Sheikh al-Mukhtar ibn Boun said in his Red Alfiyya: '(But) if you lighten it, then it is inoperative, and Yunus permits that it operates.' And from Yunus that he narrated that from the Arabs, and al-Akhfash also said it.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage presents another dialectical exchange. The questioner objects that using the lightened form of "لكن" (i.e., without nūn) to raise the subject of a nominal sentence (رفع المبتدأ) is incorrect because the lightened form is normally inoperative (أهملت). The responder defends it by citing the school of Yunus ibn Habib, a prominent grammarian, who permits it. He quotes al-Mukhtar ibn Boun's "Red Alfiyya" which states that while the general rule is that lightened "lākin" is inoperative, Yunus allows it to function. Furthermore, Yunus narrated that this usage is attested from the Arabs themselves, and al-Akhfash also held this view. This again illustrates how objections in jadal are rebutted by citing authoritative grammarian schools and reported usage.

Checkpoint

A) Sibawayh
B) Al-Akhfash
C) Yunus
D) Ibn Malik

Key Takeaway
A general grammatical rule can be challenged in jadal by citing a respected authority who allows an exception, as shown by Yunus's school on lightened 'lākin'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How would you respond using the method of citing a grammarian like Yunus or al-Akhfash? Write a brief jawab. Responding to a Grammatical Objection in Dialectic
Author rebuts objection to deriving wonder verbs and elatives from quadriliteral roots by citing Sibawayh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a dialectical exchange and evaluate the strength of a respondent's rebuttal in an Islamic grammatical debate.

Core Idea
In dialectic, a respondent can defend a claim by citing a respected authority's apparent endorsement.

In dialectical debates (munazara), the claimant (mudda'i) asserts a position, the objector (mu'tarid) raises an objection based on a general rule, and the respondent (mujib) must defend the claim. This passage from a grammar debate shows how a respondent uses the authority of Sibawayh to justify an exception to a morphological rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the positions
What is the objector's objection? What is the responder's claim? The objector says deriving wonder verbs and elatives from quadriliteral 'af'ala' violates the rule that these forms are only from triliterals. The responder claims it is permissible.
2
Find the authority cited
Which grammarian is used to support the exception? The respondent cites Sibawayh's apparent words, and also mentions Abu Ishaq and Ibn 'Usfur.
3
Understand the condition
Under what condition is the exception allowed? The respondent specifies that the hamza of 'af'ala' must not be for transitive conversion.
4
Assess the dialectical strength
Does the responder's citation sufficiently rebut the objection? The citation provides a counterexample from a primary authority, but the strength depends on whether the objector accepts Sibawayh's authority and whether the condition is met. The exchange shows a typical dialectical move: citing an exception to a rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكأن يصوغ فعلي التعجب أو صيغة التفصيل من أفعل الرباعية، فيقول: السائل هذه العبارة غلط لمخالفتها القاعدة العربية وهي أن فعلي التعجب وصيغة التفضيل لا يُصاغ شيء منهما إلا من الثلاثي. فيقول المجيب : ظاهر كلام سيبويه جواز صوغ كل ذلك من أفعل، وهو مذهب أبي إسحاق وقال به ابن عصفور، إن كانت همزة (أفعل) لغير النقل من اللزوم إلى التعدي مثلاً .
And as if forming the two verbs of wonder or the elative form from the quadriliteral 'af'ala', so the questioner says: This statement is an error because it violates the Arabic rule, which is that the two verbs of wonder and the elative form are not derived except from the triliteral. So the responder says: The apparent meaning of Sibawayh's words permits forming all of that from 'af'ala', and it is the school of Abu Ishaq, and Ibn 'Usfur held it, provided that the hamza of 'af'ala' is not for transitive conversion, for example.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage deals with morphological derivation. The questioner objects that deriving the so-called "verbs of wonder" (فعل التعجب: ما أفعله وأفعله) and the "elative" (صيغة التفضيل: أفعل) from quadriliteral verbs (like أفعل الرباعية) is invalid because these forms are restricted to triliteral roots. The responder responds by citing Sibawayh's apparent permission for such derivation from "أفعل" quadriliteral, as long as the initial hamza is not used for turning intransitive into transitive. This is also the school of Abu Ishaq and Ibn 'Usfur. This shows that the rule is not absolute and depends on the function of the hamza. The responder uses the authority of great grammarians to support the permissibility.

صيغة التفضيل
أفعل الرباعية

Key Takeaway
In dialectic, a rule-based objection can be rebutted by citing an authoritative counterexample that includes a qualifying condition.

Responding to Grammatical Objections with Samā'
Author explains how a respondent may answer grammatical objections using irregular but attested Arabic usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the dialectical rule of samā' (attested irregular usage) to respond to grammatical objections in a debate and identify the condition under which such a response is valid.

Core Idea
Samā' is a shield against grammatical objections, but only when the irregularity is preserved, not extended by qiyās.

In dialectical debates (munāẓara), the objector (mu'tariḍ) often attacks an opponent's expression by citing a standard grammatical rule. The respondent (mujīb) can defend the expression by showing that it is attested (masmū') from the Arabs, even if it violates qiyās (analogical rule). However, as our passage explains, not every irregularity can be used; the samā' must be preserved and not extended analogically. This lesson unpacks the technique and its limitations, providing concrete examples from Arabic poetry and qira'āt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's grammatical criticism
Look for the objector's claim that a specific verb form violates a standard rule — for example, using the jussive after 'lan' (which normally takes the accusative) or the accusative after 'lam' (which normally takes the jussive). The objector says: 'This expression is wrong because you made the present tense accusative with lam.'
The criticism is based solely on grammatical rule (qiyās), not on meaning.
2
Locate the respondent's defense by samā'
Find where the respondent cites a recognized grammarian (like Ibn 'Asfur) or presents poetic lines and irregular Quranic readings as evidence that the usage is attested among the Arabs, even though it deviates from the rule.
Examples include: 'ألم نشرح' with accusative and verses with jussive after 'lan', 'an', and 'law'.
3
Classify the type of samā'
Distinguish between two types: (1) a permissible derivation from an authority (like Ibn 'Asfur's view on 'yadrib' and 'yaktub'), and (2) absolute samā' that is preserved but not used as a basis for analogy (qiyās). The author warns that absolute samā' may not be a valid response.
The passage says: 'الظاهر أن مطلق السماع الذي يُحفظ ولا يقاس عليه لا يصح للمجيب الجواب به'.
4
Check the condition for valid samā' response
The author indicates that absolute samā' is only valid when the objection is solely about grammatical correctness of the expression, not about meaning or logical consistency. The respondent must not claim that the irregular form can be generalized by qiyās.
The condition limits the use of samā' to defending the attested form itself, not extending it analogically.
5
Analyze the examples provided
Review the poetic lines cited: the verse with 'lan' in the jussive by Ka'thīr, the verse with 'law' in the jussive, and the irregular reading 'ألم نشرح' with accusative. Each illustrates a form that is masmū' but contradicts standard qiyās.
These examples are specific to the Arabic grammatical tradition and are used by scholars in dialectical contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيقول المجيب : ضم راء ( يضرب) ، وكسر تاء (يكتب) جوازه جار على مذهب الإمام أبي الحسن بن عصفور، وهو من أئمة اللغة العربية، وأمثال هذا كثيرة جدا . والظاهر أن مطلق السماع الذي يُحفظ ولا يقاس عليه لا يصح للمجيب الجواب به على معترض العبارة، كما لو جزم المضارع بلن أو بلو، أو نصبه بلم، أو أثبت نون الرفع مع الجازم أو الناصب ونحو ذلك، كأن يقول : هذه العبارة خطأ ؛ لأنك نصبت المضارع بلم . فيقول المجيب : ذلك مسموع عن العرب، كقول الشاعر : في أي يومي من الموت أفرّ أيومَ لم يُقْدَرَ أم يومَ قُدِر . وقول الآخر : في كل ما هم أمضى عزمه قدما ولم يشاور في الأمر الذي فعلا . وعليه القراءة الشاذة (ألم نشرح) بالنصب، وهو قول ابن الشجري . أو : هذه العبارة خطأ؛ لأنك جزمت فيها المضارع بأن أو بلن أو بلو . فيقول المجيب : كل ذلك مسموع عن العرب، أما جزم المضارع بأن - بفتح الهمزة وتخفيف النون - فكقوله : إذاما غدونا قال ولدانُ أهلنا تعالوا إلى أن يأتِنا الصيد نحطب . وقد أجازه بعض الكوفيين ونقله اللحياني عن بعض بني صباح من ضبه . وجزمه بلن كقول كثير : أيادي سبا ياعز ما كنتُ بعدكم فلن تحل للعينين بعدك منظر . وقول الآخر : لن يخب الآن من رجائك من حرك دون بابك الحلقة . وجزمه بلو كقول الآخر : تامت فؤادك لو يحزنك ما فعلت إحدى نساء بني ذهل بن شيبانا . وقول الآخر : لويشأ طارَ بهِ ذو ميعة لاحق الآطالِ نهدٌ ذو خُصَل . والظاهر أن المحل الذي يكون للمعلل فيه الجواب عن اعتراض
The respondent says: The damma of the letter ra' (yadrib) and the kasra of the letter ta' (yaktub) — its permissibility follows the madhhab of Abu al-Hasan ibn 'Asfur, who is one of the imams of Arabic language. Examples of this are very many. And it appears that absolute samā' (heard usage) which is preserved but not used as the basis for analogy (qiyās) is not valid for the respondent to answer with it against an objector of the expression, such as if [the objector] had made the present tense jussive with lan or law, or made it accusative with lam, or affirmed the nun of raising (al-raf') with a jussive or accusative particle, and the like. For example, he says: This expression is wrong because you made the present tense accusative with lam. Then the respondent says: That is masmū' (heard) from the Arabs, as the poet said: "On which of my two days from death shall I flee — the day not decreed or the day decreed?" And the other's saying: "In every matter he decided, stepping forward, and did not consult in the matter he did." And upon it is the irregular reading of "alam nashraḥ" with accusative, which is the saying of Ibn al-Shajari. Or: This expression is wrong because you made the present tense jussive with an, lan, or law. Then the respondent says: All of that is masmū' from the Arabs. Regarding the jussive of the present with an (with fatḥa on the hamza and nun without shadda), it is like his saying: "When we set out, the sons of our people said: Come, so that the game may come to us while we gather firewood." Some of the Kufans permitted it, and al-Liḥyānī transmitted it from some of Banī Ṣubāḥ of Ḍabba. And its jussive with lan is like the saying of Kathīr: "O 'Izz, since I parted from you, the sight will never be sweet to my eyes after you." And the other's saying: "He will not now be disappointed in his hope, who has moved the ring at your door." And its jussive with law is like the other's saying: "Your heart was bewildered if what one of the women of Banī Dhuhl ibn Shaybān did grieves you." And the other's saying: "If he willed, he would be carried by one of great swiftness, with high flanks, full of strength." And it appears that the place where the mu'allil (reasoner) can answer the objection is...
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is from a text on the etiquette of dialectical debate (adab al-baḥth). It addresses a specific type of objection (i'tirāḍ) and response (jawāb) concerning grammatical expressions. The objector claims that a certain phrase is linguistically incorrect because it violates a well-known grammatical rule, such as using the jussive after 'lan' (which normally takes the accusative) or the accusative after 'lam' (which normally takes the jussive). The respondent (mujīb) may defend the expression by citing that it is permitted according to a recognized grammarian (like Ibn 'Asfur) or that it is 'masmū'' (attested from the Arabs) even though it cannot be used as a basis for analogical reasoning (qiyās). The author cautions, however, that not any 'samā'' can be used; only those forms that are preserved as exceptions and are not extended by analogy. The passage provides multiple poetic examples and mentions an irregular Quranic reading to illustrate that such forms exist in the Arabic tradition. In a dialectical context, the respondent must distinguish between rules of standard grammar (qiyās) and actual usage (samā'), and when the objector's criticism is based solely on the rule, the attested exception can be a valid response. The concluding line begins to discuss the proper context for such a response, emphasizing that it is only when the objection concerns a claim of grammatical incorrectness, not when it involves a matter of meaning or logical inconsistency.

Key Terms
المجيب (al-mujīb): The respondent in a dialectical exchange who answers the objection (i'tirāḍ) of the objector (mu'tariḍ). [Note: In later hadith terminology, mujīb can also mean the answerer of a question; here it is specific to jadal.]
المعترض (al-mu'tariḍ): The objector who raises a criticism against an expression or argument in a debate. [Note: Stable term in jadal literature; no significant shift.]
السماع (al-samā'): Attested usage from native Arab speakers, preserved and recorded, especially poetic evidence, which may deviate from standard analogical rules (qiyās). [Note: In uṣūl al-fiqh, samā' can refer to transmitted knowledge; here it specifically means linguistic attestation.]
الجزم (al-jazm): The jussive case of the present tense verb in Arabic grammar, typically used after particles like lam and lā al-nāhiya. [Note: Grammatical term; stable across the tradition.]
النصب (al-naṣb): The accusative case of the present tense verb, typically used after particles like an and lan. [Note: Stable grammatical term.]

Key Takeaway
The respondent may answer a grammatical objection by citing attested usage (samā'), but only when the objection is about correctness of expression, not meaning, and the samā' is preserved without being analogically extended.

Conditions for Valid Linguistic Evidence in Jadal
Author illustrates jussive constructions and stipulates conditions for valid linguistic evidence in debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five valid sources of linguistic evidence in jadal and apply them to evaluate an argument.

Core Idea
In jadal, every linguistic claim must be backed by imam, group, consistency, tribe, or Quranic reading.

In Islamic dialectics (jadal), debaters often rely on linguistic constructions to support their positions. But not every Arabic expression carries weight. The adab al-bahth tradition sets clear conditions for what counts as valid linguistic evidence. This passage from the classical manuals illustrates those conditions with concrete examples from poetry, tribal dialects, and Quranic readings. Understanding these criteria is essential for distinguishing a sound argument from a weak one.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the respondent's claim
The respondent begins by asserting that all listed jussive constructions are 'heard from the Arabs' (مسموع عن العرب). This establishes their existence in the language.
2
Identify the five conditions for acceptability
The author then states the conditions for a construction to be valid evidence: it must be based on (1) the saying of an imam, (2) a group of scholars, (3) consistency (mutṭarid), (4) a tribal dialect, or (5) agreement with a seven-fold Quranic reading.
3
Analyze the examples against the conditions
Examine how each example meets at least one condition. For instance, the jussive after 'an' is allowed by some Kufans (condition 2), and the raising after 'an' by ignoring it is a tribal dialect (condition 4). The Quranic readings satisfy condition 5.
4
Apply the conditions to a new argument
Given a new linguistic claim in a debate, check if it can be traced to one of the five authorities. If not, the objection against it stands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيقول المجيب : كل ذلك مسموع عن العرب، أما جزم المضارع بأن - بفتح الهمزة وتخفيف النون - فكقوله : إذاما غدونا قال ولدانُ أهلنا تعالوا إلى أن يأتِنا الصيد نحطب وقد أجازه بعض الكوفيين ونقله اللحياني عن بعض بني صباح من ضبه . وجزمه بلن كقول كثير : أيادي سبا ياعز ما كنتُ بعدكم فلن تحل للعينين بعدك منظر وقول الآخر : لن يخب الآن من رجائك من حرك دون بابك الحلقة وجزمه بلو كقول الآخر : تامت فؤادك لو يحزنك ما فعلت إحدى نساء بني ذهل بن شيبانا وقول الآخر : لويشأ طارَ بهِ ذو ميعة لاحق الآطالِ نهدٌ ذو خُصَل والظاهر أن المحل الذي يكون للمعلل فيه الجواب عن اعتراض العبارة لابد أن يكون مستندًا فيه لقول إمام، كما ذكرنا عن يونس وابن عصفور، أو طائفة كما ذكرنا عن الكوفيين أو كونه مطردًا، أو لغة لبعض القبائل، أو موافقا لقراءة سبعية مثلاً . ومثال ما هو موافق للغة بعض العرب إهمال (أن) ورفع المضارع بعدها حملاً على (ما) المصدرية، كقوله : أن تقرآن على أسماء ويحكما منى السلام وألا تشعرا أحدا وإهمال لم ورفع المضارع بعدها حملاً على ما النافية كقوله : لولا فوارس من نعم وأسرتهم يوم الصليفاء لم يوفون بالجار ومثال الموافق قراءة سبعية العطف على ضمير خفض من غير إعادة الخافض، كقراءة حمزة ﴿ وَاتَّقُوا اللهَ الَّذِي تَسَاءَلُونَ بِهِ وَالْأَرْحَامِ ﴾ [النساء/ ١] بخفض (الأرحام) معطوفا على الضمير المخفوض وكالفصل بين المضاف والمضاف إليه بمفعول المضاف، كقراءة ابن عامر الله وكذالك زَيَّنَ لِكَثِيرِ مِنَ الْمُشْرِكِينَ قَتْلَ أَوْلَدِهِمْ شُرَكَاؤُهُمْ ﴾ [الأنعام/ ١٣٧] ببناء (زين) للمفعول. ونصب (أولادهم)
The respondent says: All that is heard from the Arabs. As for the jussive of the present tense after 'an' (with fatha on the hamza and lightening of the nun), it is like his saying: 'When we set out, the sons of our family said: Come so that the game may come to us, we gather firewood.' And some of the Kufans allowed it, and al-Lihyani transmitted it from some of Banu Sabah from Dubba. And its jussive after 'lan' is like the saying of Kathir: 'O 'Izz, I was not with you after you [left]; the sight will not be sweet to the eyes after you.' And the saying of another: 'Now he will not fail, from his hope in you, he who moves the ring at your door.' And its jussive after 'law' is like the saying of another: 'Your heart was wounded, if what one of the women of Banu Dhuhl ibn Shayban did would sadden you.' And the saying of another: 'If he wished, a swift, high-spirited, long-maned, sturdy horse with tufts would fly with him.' And it appears that the place where the arguer has the answer to the objection of the expression must be based on the saying of an imam, as we mentioned from Yunus and Ibn 'Usfur, or a group as we mentioned from the Kufans, or it being consistent, or a language of some tribes, or being in agreement with a seven-fold reading for example. And an example of what agrees with the language of some Arabs is the ignoring of 'an' and the raising of the present tense after it by analogy with the gerundial 'ma', like his saying: 'That you two read to Asma' and pass judgment from me with peace, and that you do not inform anyone.' And the ignoring of 'lam' and raising of the present tense after it by analogy with the negative 'ma' like his saying: 'Were it not for the horsemen of Na'm and their families, on the day of al-Sulayfa', they would not have fulfilled (their duty) to the neighbor.' And an example of agreement with a seven-fold reading is the conjunction on a genitive pronoun without repeating the preposition, like the reading of Hamza: 'Fear Allah, through whom you ask one another, and the wombs' [al-Nisa' 4:1] with the genitive of 'al-arham' as a conjunction on the genitive pronoun. And like the separation between the construct and the constructed-to by the object of the construct, like the reading of Ibn 'Amir: 'And thus their associates have made pleasing to many of the polytheists the killing of their children' [al-An'am 6:137] with the passive construction of 'zayyana' and the accusative of 'awladahum'.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical expansion showing how linguistic evidence is used in jadal (debate). The author begins by stating that all the cited jussive constructions are actually heard from Arabs, thus establishing their legitimacy. He provides examples of the present tense verb in the jussive after 'an', 'lan', and 'law', quoting lines of poetry. After these examples, he pivots to the key point: when an arguer answers an objection concerning a linguistic expression, his answer must be based on one of four types of authority: (1) the saying of an imam (e.g., Yunus or Ibn 'Usfur), (2) the saying of a group (e.g., the Kufans), (3) consistency of usage (i.e., the construction is regular), (4) a tribal dialect, or (5) agreement with a seven-fold Quranic reading. The author then gives further examples: ignoring 'an' (so it does not govern the subjunctive) and raising the verb after it by analogy with the gerundial 'ma'; ignoring 'lam' (so it does not govern the jussive) and raising the verb after it by analogy with the negative 'ma'; and finally two examples from Quranic readings: the conjunction of 'al-arham' on the genitive pronoun in the Hamza reading, and the separation between mudaf and mudaf ilayh by the object in the Ibn 'Amir reading. The passage thus teaches that in jadal, every linguistic claim must be verified by solid evidence from the Arabic language tradition, not merely personal opinion.

Key Terms
جزم
إهمال
مطرد
قراءة سبعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Agreement with a seven-fold Quranic reading

Key Takeaway
The validity of linguistic evidence in jadal depends on its backing by recognized authorities: imam, group, consistency, tribal dialect, or Quranic reading.

Valid Bases for Linguistic Evidence in Jadal
Author lists valid bases for linguistic evidence and gives examples from Arabic grammar and qira'at.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which bases for linguistic evidence are valid in argumentation according to Adab al-Bahth and distinguish them from unrestricted transmitted usage.

Core Idea
Not all transmitted usage is valid; only that backed by an imam, a school, consistency, a tribal dialect, or a qira'a.

In dialectical argumentation (jadal), a disputant often uses linguistic expressions as evidence. But not every usage is acceptable. The author of Adab al-Bahth lays out five valid bases for accepting a linguistic expression. Understanding these helps you evaluate whether a linguistic objection is sound and whether a disputant's response is legitimate.

Guided Path
1
List the valid bases
Identify the five bases the author mentions: saying of an imam, group/school, consistency (muṭṭarid), tribal language, and agreement with a sevenfold qira'a.
The imams cited are Yunus and Ibn 'Usfur; the group is the Kufans.
2
Examine the poetic examples
Look at the two lines of poetry: how they illustrate ignoring 'an' or 'lam' and raising the present tense verb. What standard rule do they violate? What makes them acceptable (tribal dialect, or analogical with 'ma' masdariyya)?
The first line: 'أن تقرآن...' with raised verb; second: 'لم يوفون بالجار' with raised verb after lam.
3
Analyze the qira'a examples
Examine Hamza's recitation (وَاتَّقُوا اللهَ... وَالْأَرْحَامِ) and Ibn 'Amir's (قتل أولادهم شركائهم). How do they deviate from standard grammar? What authorizes them (canonical qira'a, even if unconventional)?
Hamza's reading involves conjunction on a genitive pronoun; Ibn 'Amir's separates construct and constructee.
4
Distinguish valid from invalid samā'
Note the author's conclusion: unrestricted transmitted usage (mutlaq al-samā') is not a valid answer. Only the listed bases count. Why? Because isolated transmissions are not reliable for argumentation.
This is the key rule: not every rare usage can be cited.
5
Apply the criteria
Given a new expression, determine which valid basis it falls under, or if it is merely unrestricted samā'. For example, a Hadith with a rare grammatical form – is it from a consistent pattern or just an isolated narration?
Practice with examples from the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العبارة لابد أن يكون مستندًا فيه لقول إمام، كما ذكرنا عن يونس وابن عصفور، أو طائفة كما ذكرنا عن الكوفيين أو كونه مطردًا، أو لغة لبعض القبائل، أو موافقا لقراءة سبعية مثلاً . ومثال ما هو موافق للغة بعض العرب إهمال أن ورفع المضارع بعدها حملاً على ما المصدرية، كقوله : أن تقرآن على أسماء ويحكما منى السلام وألا تشعرا أحدا وإهمال لم ورفع المضارع بعدها حملاً على ما النافية كقوله : لولا فوارس من نعم وأسرتهم يوم الصليفاء لم يوفون بالجار ومثال الموافق القراءة سبعية العطف على ضمير خفض من غير إعادة الخافض، كقراءة حمزة ﴿ وَاتَّقُوا اللهَ الَّذِي تَسَاءَلُونَ بِهِ وَالْأَرْحَامِ ﴾ بخفض الأرحام معطوفا على الضمير المخفوض وكالفصل بين المضاف والمضاف إليه بمفعول المضاف، كقراءة ابن عامر وكذالك زَيَّنَ لِكَثِيرِ مِنَ الْمُشْرِكِينَ قَتْلَ أَوْلَدِهِمْ شُرَكَاؤُهُمْ ببناء زين للمفعول. ونصب أولادهم، وخفض شركائهم ؛ على أن لفظة قتل مضاف وشركائهم مضاف إليه، وأولادهم منصوب هو مفعول المضاف، وقد حال بين المضاف والمضاف إليه . فمثل هذه الأشياء يصح بها للمعلل الجواب عن فساد العبارة بمخالفتها للقواعد العربية، والظاهر أن مطلق السماع لا يصح أن يكون جوابًا، وأمثلة النوعين كثيرة جدا في كلام العرب، ولكن لا نطيل الكلام بذلك زيادة على ما ذكرنا .
The expression must be based on the saying of an imam, as we mentioned from Yunus and Ibn 'Usfur, or a group as we mentioned from the Kufans, or it being consistent, or the language of some tribes, or agreeing with a sevenfold qira'a for example. And an example of what agrees with the language of some Arabs is ignoring 'an' and raising the present tense verb after it based on 'ma' masdariyya, like his saying: 'an taqra'an 'ala Asma'a wa yahkuma minni al-salam wa alla tash'ura ahadan'. And ignoring 'lam' and raising the present tense verb after it based on the negative 'ma', like his saying: 'lawla fawaris min Na'm wa asratihim yawm al-sulayfa'i lam yawfu bi al-jar'. And an example of agreeing with a sevenfold qira'a is the conjunction on a genitive pronoun without repeating the preposition, like Hamza's recitation: 'And fear Allah, through whom you ask, and the wombs' with lowering of 'al-arham' as a conjunction on the lowered pronoun. And like the separation between the construct and the constructee by the object of the construct, as in Ibn 'Amir's recitation: 'And thus their partners have made pleasing to many of the polytheists the killing of their children' with 'zuyyina' built for the passive, and 'awlādahum' in the accusative, and 'shurakā'uhum' in the genitive; such that the word 'qatl' is the construct and 'shurakā'ihim' is the constructee, and 'awlādahum' in the accusative is the object of the construct, and it has intervened between the construct and constructee. Such things make it valid for the arguer to answer regarding the corruption of the expression due to its violation of Arabic rules. And it appears that unrestricted transmitted usage is not valid as an answer. And examples of both types are very many in the speech of Arabs, but we will not lengthen the discussion beyond what we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage from Adab al-Bahth discusses the conditions under which a linguistic expression (العبارة) can be used as evidence in dialectical argumentation. The author begins by stating that the expression must be based on a recognized authority or consistent rule. Specifically, it must rely on the saying of an imam (like Yunus or Ibn 'Usfur), the consensus of a school (like the Kufans), a consistent pattern (مطرد), the dialect of a tribe, or agreement with a canonical qira'a. The author then provides concrete examples. The first example is ignoring the particle 'an' and raising the verb after it, as in the line of poetry 'أن تقرآن...' This usage is justified by analogy with 'ma' masdariyya. The second example is ignoring 'lam' and raising the verb after it, as in the line 'لم يوفون بالجار'. These illustrate tribal linguistic usage. The third example is from the Quran: Hamza's reading of 'والأرحام' with a genitive case, which is a conjunction on a genitive pronoun without repeating the preposition. The fourth example is Ibn 'Amir's reading of 'قتل أولادهم شركائهم', which separates the construct (قتل) from its constructee (شركائهم) by the object (أولادهم). The author concludes that such usages allow the disputant to counter objections that the expression violates Arabic rules. However, he emphasizes that unrestricted transmitted usage (مطلق السماع) is not a valid defense; only those based on the above criteria are acceptable. The passage ends by noting that examples are abundant.

مستند
مطرد
السماع
المعلل

D) Consistency (اطراد) in the language

Key Takeaway
Valid linguistic evidence in jadal must be rooted in an imam's saying, a school's practice, consistency, a tribal dialect, or a canonical qira'a; isolated transmissions are insufficient.

Answering the Objection of Corrupt Expression
Author explains how to respond to objections of violating Arabic grammar rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to respond to an objection of fasad al-'ibara in a debate by citing analogous permissible Arabic constructions.

Core Idea
Grammatical violations in debate are answered by showing the rule permits the construction.

In dialectical exchanges, an opponent may object that your expression violates Arabic grammatical rules. This is called fasad al-'ibara. The author provides a method to counter such objections: not by denying the rule, but by demonstrating through analogous examples that the construction is actually acceptable in Arabic speech. This lesson unpacks that tactic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Determine what exactly is being claimed as corrupt expression. For example, the objector may say the word order or case marking violates a known grammatical rule.
In the passage, the objection involves a construct phrase where the object appears separated from the first term.
2
Determine the grammatical rule invoked
State explicitly which rule the objector relies on. This helps you target your response.
The rule here is about idafa: the second term must be in genitive and the first term must not have the object inserted between them.
3
Find analogous permissible constructions
Search your knowledge of Arabic (Qur'an, hadith, poetry, common usage) for similar structures that are accepted. The author notes that examples are abundant.
For instance, the Qur'anic verse 'wa-khafdi shurakaihim' itself is such an example.
4
Construct your response
Present the analogous example and argue that your expression follows the same pattern, thus it is not a violation. The burden is on the objector to show why your analogy fails.
The author warns that mere appeal to absolute usage (sama') without analogy is insufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخفض ( شركائهم ) ؛ على أن لفظة (قتل) مضاف وشركائهم) مضاف إليه، و(أولادهم) منصوب هو مفعول المضاف، وقد حال بين المضاف والمضاف إليه . فمثل هذه الأشياء يصح بها للمعلل الجواب عن فساد العبارة بمخالفتها للقواعد العربية، والظاهر أن مطلق السماع لا يصح أن يكون جوابًا، وأمثلة النوعين كثيرة جدا في كلام العرب، ولكن لا نطيل الكلام بذلك زيادة على ما ذكرنا .
And lowering (their partners); because the word (killing) is the first part of a construct and (their partners) is the second part, and (their children) is accusative object of the first part, and it has come between the first part and the second part. So such things enable the arguer to answer the corruption of expression by violating Arabic grammatical rules, and it appears that absolute reliance on usage is not valid as an answer, and examples of both types are very many in Arabic speech, but we will not lengthen the speech beyond what we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage discusses how an arguer in a debate can respond to an objection that his statement is grammatically corrupt (fasad al-'ibara). The author gives a specific linguistic example: the phrase 'wa-khafdi shurakaihim' (and lowering their partners) where 'shurakaihim' is in genitive as the second term of a construct, and 'awladahum' is accusative as the object of the construct, even though it is separated from the construct by the possessive. Such constructions, the author explains, can be used by the arguer to counter the claim that his expression violates Arabic grammar. The key principle is that merely pointing out a grammatical violation is not sufficient; the arguer must demonstrate that the expression is actually permissible by citing analogous examples from the Arabic language. The author notes that examples of both types (the violation and the permissible usage) are abundant in Arabic, but he will not elaborate further. This is a practical tactic in the art of debate (jadal) for rebutting objections concerning linguistic form.

Key Takeaway
To rebut a grammatical objection, cite analogous Arabic constructions that follow the same pattern, not just appeal to general usage.
